The Four Essential Attributes of Prophets
Defines the four obligatory attributes of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four attributes that are obligatory for all prophets according to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Prophets are distinguished by four essential traits: truthfulness, trustworthiness, conveyance, and intelligence.

The author first establishes the unique status of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and then lists the four qualities that every prophet must possess. These attributes form the basis for trusting in prophetic messages.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on prophetic attributes
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the unified testimony to the listing of attributes.
The passage begins by noting that the two testimonies have become one word.
2
Understand the definition of each attribute
For each of the four attributes (sidq, amana, tabligh, fatana), identify its definition as given in the passage. Pay attention to how they are described.
Some equate trustworthiness with infallibility (isma).
3
Recognize the obligatory nature of these attributes
The passage states that these four are obligatory (wajib) for prophets. Think about why these specific qualities are necessary for their role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) ورئيسهم والفائق عليهم بالفضائل والكمالات (۳) ، فصارت الكلمتان كلمة واحدة، عبر عنها بكلمة التوحيد والإخلاص (٤) أي المتعلقة بهما بعد أن خصه كما خص اخوانه من الأنبياء والرسل الكرام بالصدق والأمانة والتبليغ والفطانه ، فهذه أربع صفات تجب في حقهم .. المعبر فالصدق هو الإخبار بالحق الثابت في نفس الأمر ، أي كون ما بلغوا به عن الله تعالى موافقا لما عند الله تعالى إيجابا كان أو سلباً . والأمانة : كونهم لا تصدر عنهم مخالفه أصلا ، وهي عند بعضهم بالعصمة . والتبليغ : هو أنهم بلغوا جميع ما أمروا به اعتقاديا كان أو عملياً ، ولم يكتموا منه شيئا . والفطانه : هي التيقظ لإلزام الخصوم وطرق إبطال تحيلهم ودعاويهم الباطلة (٥) وفي ضمن ذلك اعتقاد حقيقة الموت ، وإبتلائه به ، كل ذي روح لأنه من مجوزات العقول التي ورد الشرع بها ، فوجب إعتقادها ، وهو كيفية وجودية تضاد الحياة ، فلا يعرى الجسم الحيواني عنهما ولا يجتمعان فيه هذا قول الأشعري . وقيل : عدم الحياة عما من شأنه الحياة ، وهو قول الاسفرايني والأكثرين وقال بعض الصوفية : ليس الموت بعدم محض ولا فناء صرف ، وإنما هو إنقطاع تعلق الروح بالبدن ومفارقته ، وحيلولة بينهما ، وتبدل بحال وانتقال من دار إلى دار .
(2) And he is their chief and superior over them in virtues and perfections (3), so the two words became one word, expressed by the word of tawhid and ikhlas (4), meaning concerning them both, after He distinguished him as He distinguished his brothers among the prophets and noble messengers with truthfulness, trustworthiness, conveyance, and intelligence. So these are four attributes obligatory upon them. The expression: truthfulness (sidq) is informing of the truth established in the matter itself, meaning that what they convey from Allah Almighty corresponds to what is with Allah Almighty, whether affirmative or negative. Trustworthiness (amana): that no violation issues from them at all, and according to some it is infallibility (isma). Conveyance (tabligh): that they conveyed all that they were commanded, whether creedal or practical, and did not conceal any of it. Intelligence (fatana): it is vigilance to confound opponents and ways of invalidating their tricks and false claims (5). And within that is the belief in the reality of death, and His afflicting with it every possessor of a soul, because it is among what is intellectually possible (mujawwizat al-uqul) that the revealed law has conveyed, so it is obligatory to believe it. And it is an existential quality that opposes life, so the animal body is not free of both, nor do they combine in it. This is the view of al-Ash'ari. And it was said: it is the absence of life from what is capable of life, which is the view of al-Isfara'ini and the majority. And some Sufis said: death is neither pure non-existence nor mere annihilation, but rather it is the severance of the soul’s attachment to the body and its separation, a barrier between them, a change of state, and a transition from one abode to another.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the four attributes that are obligatory for prophets and then presents different theological views on death. It begins by further praising the Prophet's superiority, then states that the two testimonies (tawhid and risala) have become one word. It then lists the four essential qualities of prophets: truthfulness (sidq) – perfect correspondence between their message and divine reality; trustworthiness (amana) – they never commit violations, equated with infallibility (isma) by some; conveyance (tabligh) – they deliver everything commanded, both creedal and practical; and intelligence (fatana) – alertness to refute opponents and expose false claims. The passage then transitions to the belief in death, asserting it is obligatory to believe in its reality because it is intellectually possible (mujawwaz) and conveyed by revelation. Three positions are given: the Ash'ari view that death is an existential quality opposed to life, and body cannot be without both simultaneously; the Isfara'ini and majority view that it is the absence of life from a living being; and a Sufi view that it is not extinction but the disconnection of the soul from the body and transition to another abode.

Key Terms
الصدق
الأمانة
التبليغ
الفطانه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'fatana' (intelligence) refers to worldly cunning or deceit.
Correction: Fatana is specifically prophetic insight and alertness to defend the truth, not cunning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of sidq (truthfulness)?
A) Informing of what benefits the community
B) Informing of the truth established in the matter itself
C) Not lying under any circumstance
D) Conveying the entire message

Key Takeaway
Prophets must possess truthfulness, trustworthiness, conveyance, and intelligence as essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four prophetic attributes do you find most challenging to embody in your own life? What is one concrete step you can take this week to cultivate that quality in your dealings with others?
?
The prophets delivered the entire message without concealment, even when it was difficult. Is there any part of your faith that you downplay or hide to avoid discomfort? What would true tabligh look like in your context?
?
Fatana includes sharpness to refute falsehood. Do you actively seek knowledge to defend your beliefs when they are challenged, or do you remain silent? What book or topic will you study next to strengthen your intellectual defense of the truth?

The Reality of Death: Three Theological Views
Explains three theological views on the nature of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological perspectives on the nature of death presented by al-Ash'ari, al-Isfara'ini, and some Sufis.

Core Idea
Death is not simple non-existence but a real transition debated among theologians.

After discussing prophetic attributes, the author turns to a related belief: the reality of death. He presents three distinct views: the Ash'ari position that death is an existential quality opposing life, the majority view that it is the absence of life, and a Sufi perspective that it is the soul's separation from the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on death
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the author frames belief in death as obligatory.
The passage begins with 'wa fi dhalika i'tiqad haqiqat al-mawt'.
2
Identify the three views and their proponents
Find each named view: al-Ash'ari, al-Isfara'ini (and the majority), and some Sufis. Note the definition each gives.
The Sufi view is explicitly not pure non-existence.
3
Understand why belief in death is obligatory
The passage says death is 'min mujawwizat al-uqul' (intellectually possible) and confirmed by revelation, so belief is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) ورئيسهم والفائق عليهم بالفضائل والكمالات (۳) ، فصارت الكلمتان كلمة واحدة، عبر عنها بكلمة التوحيد والإخلاص (٤) أي المتعلقة بهما بعد أن خصه كما خص اخوانه من الأنبياء والرسل الكرام بالصدق والأمانة والتبليغ والفطانه ، فهذه أربع صفات تجب في حقهم .. المعبر فالصدق هو الإخبار بالحق الثابت في نفس الأمر ، أي كون ما بلغوا به عن الله تعالى موافقا لما عند الله تعالى إيجابا كان أو سلباً . والأمانة : كونهم لا تصدر عنهم مخالفه أصلا ، وهي عند بعضهم بالعصمة . والتبليغ : هو أنهم بلغوا جميع ما أمروا به اعتقاديا كان أو عملياً ، ولم يكتموا منه شيئا . والفطانه : هي التيقظ لإلزام الخصوم وطرق إبطال تحيلهم ودعاويهم الباطلة (٥) وفي ضمن ذلك اعتقاد حقيقة الموت ، وإبتلائه به ، كل ذي روح لأنه من مجوزات العقول التي ورد الشرع بها ، فوجب إعتقادها ، وهو كيفية وجودية تضاد الحياة ، فلا يعرى الجسم الحيواني عنهما ولا يجتمعان فيه هذا قول الأشعري . وقيل : عدم الحياة عما من شأنه الحياة ، وهو قول الاسفرايني والأكثرين وقال بعض الصوفية : ليس الموت بعدم محض ولا فناء صرف ، وإنما هو إنقطاع تعلق الروح بالبدن ومفارقته ، وحيلولة بينهما ، وتبدل بحال وانتقال من دار إلى دار .
(2) And he is their chief and superior over them in virtues and perfections (3), so the two words became one word, expressed by the word of tawhid and ikhlas (4), meaning concerning them both, after He distinguished him as He distinguished his brothers among the prophets and noble messengers with truthfulness, trustworthiness, conveyance, and intelligence. So these are four attributes obligatory upon them. The expression: truthfulness (sidq) is informing of the truth established in the matter itself, meaning that what they convey from Allah Almighty corresponds to what is with Allah Almighty, whether affirmative or negative. Trustworthiness (amana): that no violation issues from them at all, and according to some it is infallibility (isma). Conveyance (tabligh): that they conveyed all that they were commanded, whether creedal or practical, and did not conceal any of it. Intelligence (fatana): it is vigilance to confound opponents and ways of invalidating their tricks and false claims (5). And within that is the belief in the reality of death, and His afflicting with it every possessor of a soul, because it is among what is intellectually possible (mujawwizat al-uqul) that the revealed law has conveyed, so it is obligatory to believe it. And it is an existential quality that opposes life, so the animal body is not free of both, nor do they combine in it. This is the view of al-Ash'ari. And it was said: it is the absence of life from what is capable of life, which is the view of al-Isfara'ini and the majority. And some Sufis said: death is neither pure non-existence nor mere annihilation, but rather it is the severance of the soul’s attachment to the body and its separation, a barrier between them, a change of state, and a transition from one abode to another.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the four attributes that are obligatory for prophets and then presents different theological views on death. It begins by further praising the Prophet's superiority, then states that the two testimonies (tawhid and risala) have become one word. It then lists the four essential qualities of prophets: truthfulness (sidq) – perfect correspondence between their message and divine reality; trustworthiness (amana) – they never commit violations, equated with infallibility (isma) by some; conveyance (tabligh) – they deliver everything commanded, both creedal and practical; and intelligence (fatana) – alertness to refute opponents and expose false claims. The passage then transitions to the belief in death, asserting it is obligatory to believe in its reality because it is intellectually possible (mujawwaz) and conveyed by revelation. Three positions are given: the Ash'ari view that death is an existential quality opposed to life, and body cannot be without both simultaneously; the Isfara'ini and majority view that it is the absence of life from a living being; and a Sufi view that it is not extinction but the disconnection of the soul from the body and transition to another abode.

Key Terms
الموت
كيفية وجودية
عدم الحياة
تعلق الروح بالبدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think death is absolute non-existence or annihilation.
Correction: The Sufi view explicitly denies this, and even the majority view defines it as absence of life, not non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is death?
A) Absence of life
B) An existential quality that opposes life
C) Severance of soul from body
D) Pure non-existence

Key Takeaway
The Islamic tradition offers multiple valid interpretations of death as a real transition, not mere annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sufi view says death is not pure non-existence but a transition to another abode. If that is true, what are you actively doing right now to prepare for that transition? Write one concrete action you will take today because of this belief.
?
Al-Ash'ari says death is an existential quality that opposes life and is never absent from a living body. Do you live as if death is a distant event or as a constant reality? What would change if you truly acknowledged death's presence alongside life every moment?
?
The majority view defines death as the absence of life from that which is capable of life. You have life now. If death were to take it away this evening, what is one thing you would regret not having done differently? Commit to doing that today.

Shafi'i on the Uncreated Quran and Takfir
Narrative of al-Shafi'i's argument and takfir of Hafs al-Fard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the dispute over the createdness of God's speech and the severe consequences of theological disagreement as exemplified by Imam al-Shafi'i's declaration of takfir.

Core Idea
Denying the uncreatedness of God's speech was considered an act of unbelief by Imam al-Shafi'i.

The question of whether the Quran is created or uncreated was one of the most divisive theological issues in early Islam. This narration places Imam al-Shafi'i firmly on the side of the uncreatedness of God's speech. It also illustrates the seriousness with which such doctrinal differences were treated, as Shafi'i declared his opponent an unbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the direct confrontation and the strong language used.
The Arabic uses 'كفر' which means 'he declared an unbeliever'.
2
Identify the theological stance
What did al-Shafi'i argue about God's speech? Find the phrase 'غير مخلوق' (uncreated).
3
Understand the stakes
Consider why al-Shafi'i would go so far as to declare Hafs an unbeliever. Reflect on the importance of this doctrine.
Hafs later said al-Shafi'i wanted to kill him, showing the extreme tension.
4
Reflect on takfir
Takfir (declaring someone an unbeliever) is a severe act. Ask yourself: What does this tell us about the centrality of this belief?
This incident is a historical example of takfir based on a doctrinal issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضرت الشافعي، وحفص الفرد سأل الشافعي، فاحتج عليه بأن كلام الله غير مخلوق، وكفر حفص المنفرد، قال الربيع: ولقيته فقال أراد الشافعي قتلي.
I was present with al-Shafi'i, and Hafs al-Fard questioned al-Shafi'i, so he argued against him that the speech of God is uncreated, and he declared Hafs al-Munfarid an unbeliever. Al-Rabi' said: I met him and he said: al-Shafi'i wanted to kill me.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This narration reports a direct theological confrontation. Hafs al-Fard asked Shafi'i a question, and Shafi'i responded by arguing that God's speech (i.e., the Quran) is uncreated (غير مخلوق). He then declared Hafs an unbeliever (كفر حفص). The report adds that Hafs later told Al-Rabi' that Shafi'i wanted to kill him, indicating the extreme tension. This incident highlights the central issue of the createdness of the Quran and shows Shafi'i's firm stance that denying its uncreatedness is tantamount to unbelief.

Key Terms
كلام الله
غير مخلوق
تكفير
حفص الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute was merely a personal or political feud.
Correction: The disagreement was fundamentally doctrinal, centered on the nature of God's speech, and al-Shafi'i's takfir shows the gravity of the theological issue.

Checkpoint
What did al-Shafi'i argue about God's speech in this dispute?
A) It is created.
B) It is uncreated.
C) It is metaphorical.
D) It is unknown.

Key Takeaway
For Imam al-Shafi'i, the uncreatedness of God's speech was a non-negotiable doctrine, and opposing it warranted being declared an unbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah, or have you allowed doubts or contemporary views to weaken this conviction? If asked to defend it, could you state it as firmly as al-Shafi'i did?
?
When you encounter someone who denies a core attribute of Allah, like His speech, do you feel the same urgency and seriousness that al-Shafi'i displayed, or do you dismiss it as a minor disagreement? How would your response change if you truly believed their soul is at stake?
?
Al-Shafi'i was willing to go to extreme lengths—even conflict—to preserve the doctrine of the uncreated Quran. What is one area of your faith where you are unwilling to compromise, even if it means losing relationships or comfort? Are you ready to face that cost today?

Concluding Beliefs of the Third Pillar on Divine Will and Prophethood
A list of concluding theological beliefs on divine will, obligation, and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final set of beliefs that conclude the third pillar of Ghazali's creed, covering divine will, human obligation, and prophethood.

Core Idea
God's will is absolute; He is not bound by human notions of benefit or obligation.

This passage brings together the concluding propositions of the third pillar. It affirms that all human actions are subject to God's will, yet He is not constrained to choose what seems most beneficial to us. The passage also establishes the possibility of prophethood and confirms the prophetic miracles of Muhammad (peace be upon him). These statements encapsulate key Ash'ari positions on divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it is a list of distinct statements concluding a larger discussion.
The passage begins mid-sentence ('للعباد'), indicating it is a continuation.
2
Identify the main themes
Group the statements into themes: divine will and creation (first two clauses), divine sovereignty in obligation (next three clauses), and prophethood (last two clauses).
3
Examine the first theme: divine will and creation
Consider what it means that actions are willed by God and that He is gracious in creation. How does this affirm God's control over all things?
This rejects the idea that human actions are outside God's will.
4
Reflect on the second theme: God's absolute sovereignty
Look at the statements about imposing unbearable tasks, inflicting pain, and no obligation to choose the most beneficial. Why does Ghazali include these? They emphasize that God is not bound by human concepts of fairness.
This is a core Ash'ari doctrine.
5
Consider the third theme: prophethood
Why is sending prophets permissible? How does the passage confirm Muhammad's prophethood through miracles? What is the significance of the footnote?
The footnote ties back to earlier pillars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للعباد ، وأنها مرادة الله تعالى ، وأنه متفضل بالخلق والإختراع وأن له تعالى تكليف ما لا يطاق ، وأن له إيلام البريء ولا يجب عليه رعاية الأصلح وأنه لا واجب إلا بالشرع ، وأن بعثه الأنبياء جائز وأن نبوة نبينا محمد ثابتة مؤيدة بالمعجزات (۱)
(۱) بالمعجزات الباهرة ، ثم إن هذه الأركان الثلاثة التي تقدم ذكرها في الإلهيات والنبوات
For the servants, and that they are willed by Allah Almighty, and that He is gracious in creation and invention, and that He may impose what is unbearable, and that He may inflict pain on the innocent and is not obliged to observe what is most beneficial, and that there is no obligation except by divine law, and that sending prophets is permissible and that the prophethood of our Prophet Muhammad is established and supported by miracles (1). (1) By dazzling miracles, then that these three pillars which were mentioned earlier in the divine matters and prophecies.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a set of theological propositions concluding the third pillar of the work. It begins by asserting that human actions are willed by God, that God is gracious in creation, and that He can impose what is unbearable. It also states that God may inflict pain on the innocent without being obligated to choose the most beneficial option, and that obligations only arise through divine law. Then it affirms that sending prophets is possible and that Muhammad's prophethood is established by miracles. The footnote (1) adds that these miracles are dazzling and notes that these three pillars (the first three) were already discussed in the sections on divinity and prophethood.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الخلق
التكليف
إيلام البريء
الأصلح
الشرع
النبوة
المعجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human actions are not willed by God or that God is obligated to always choose the most beneficial for His creatures.
Correction: The passage affirms that all actions are willed by God and that He is not obliged to observe what is most beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is a necessary characteristic of God regarding human actions?
A) He is obligated to choose the most beneficial for His creatures.
B) He may impose what is unbearable.
C) He must always refrain from inflicting pain.
D) He is not all-knowing.

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolute; He is not bound by human notions of benefit, and prophethood is confirmed by miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that Allah may impose on you what is unbearable, or do you secretly believe that your trials must be within your limits? How does this belief shape your response when life feels overwhelming?
?
If Allah is not obligated to choose what is most beneficial for you, are you still able to trust His choices completely, even when they seem harmful or unfair? Examine your heart: what would it take for you to surrender that expectation?
?
Since no obligation exists except by divine law, are you living by standards that come from revelation or from your own desires and society? Identify one area where you have placed human judgment above Allah's command, and commit to realigning it today.

The First Pillar and the Three Categories of Divine Attributes
Ghazali introduces the first pillar; Ibn al-Humam classifies attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational classification of divine attributes into obligatory, impossible, and possible, and the first principle of knowledge of God's existence.

Core Idea
The knowledge of God begins with His existence and the three rational categories of attributes.

This lesson opens the discussion of the first pillar of faith: knowledge of God. Imam Ghazali introduces the ten principles, beginning with knowledge of God's existence. Ibn al-Humam then clarifies that this knowledge is straightforward and presents the three fundamental categories of divine attributes that underlie all theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's introduction
Read the opening lines where Ghazali states the first pillar and its ten principles. Note the emphasis on knowledge of God's existence.
The passage includes a footnote marker for Ibn al-Humam's commentary.
2
Understand Ibn al-Humam's clarification
Read Ibn al-Humam's note that knowledge of existence is easy because 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous. This corrects any notion that such knowledge is esoteric.
3
Identify the three categories
List the three types of divine matters: obligatory (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz). Recognize that these categories are the framework for discussing all divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الركن الأول من أركان الإيمان في معرفة ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى ، وإن الله تعالى واحد ومداره على عشرة أصول
الأصل الأول : العلم بوجوده تعالى ومعرفة وجوده (۱) تعالى
وأول ما يستضاء به من الأنوار ، ويسلك من طريق الإعتبار، ما أرشد إليه
(۱) وعبارة ابن الهمام في المسايرة : العلم بوجوده تعالى ، وهو سهل ، لأن العلم والمعرفة لغة شيء واحد ؛ واعلم أن الإلهيات المبحوث فيها عن الإله جل وعز أنواع ثلاثة :
الأول : فيما يجب الله عز وجل ، الثاني : فيما يستحيل في حقه تعالى : الثالث : فيما يجوز في حقه تعالى النوع الأول : فيما يجب له تعالى ، فمما يجب له تعالى عشرون صفة وهل صفاته تعالى تنحصر في هذه العشرين أم لا ، والصحيح أنها تابعة لكمالاته وكمالاته لا نهاية لها لكن العجز عن معرفة ما لم ينصب لنا عليه دليل عقلي ولا نقلي ولا نؤاخذ به بفضل الله تعالى ، ومفهومه أن ما قام عليه الدليل نؤاخذ بتركه وهي هذه العشرون صفة ، ومعنى كمالاته لا نهاية لها ، هل هو بإعتبار علمنا ، أو بإعتبار علم الله تعالى ؟ أما بإعتبار علمنا فظاهر لنقصه وضعفه وأما بإعتبار علم الله فمعناه علمها على ما هي عليه من عدم النهاية
ويحتمل أن تكون لا نهاية لها بإعتبار لغة العرب ، لأن العرب إذ أكثر الشيء يحكمون عليه بعدم النهاية وإن كان في نفسه متناهيا ،
ويحتمل أن تكون حكم عليها بعدم النهاية مراعاة للنفسية والسلبية ، لأنها لا نهاية لها . وأما المعاني
As for the first pillar of the pillars of faith, it is about knowledge of the essence of God, Glorified and Exalted, and that God Almighty is One, and its pivot is upon ten principles. The first principle: knowledge of His existence, Exalted, and knowing His existence (1) Exalted. And the first thing by which lights are illuminated, and by which the path of reflection is traversed, is that to which guidance is given. (1) The expression of Ibn al-Humam in al-Musayara: knowledge of His existence, Exalted, and it is easy, because knowledge and cognition are one thing linguistically; know that the divine matters investigated about the deity, Mighty and Majestic, are of three types: the first: what is obligatory for God, Mighty and Majestic; the second: what is impossible in His right, Exalted; the third: what is possible in His right, Exalted. The first type: what is obligatory for Him, Exalted. Among what is obligatory for Him, Exalted, are twenty attributes. And whether His attributes, Exalted, are limited to these twenty or not, and the correct view is that they are dependent on His perfections, and His perfections have no end, but disability from knowing what no rational or transmitted proof has been set up for us, and we are not taken to task for it by God's grace. And its meaning is that what proof has been established for, we are taken to task for abandoning it, and they are these twenty attributes. And the meaning of 'His perfections have no end' — is it with regard to our knowledge or with regard to God's knowledge? As for with regard to our knowledge, it is apparent due to its deficiency and weakness. As for with regard to God's knowledge, its meaning is His knowledge of them as they are in their lack of end. And it is possible that they have no end with regard to the language of the Arabs, because when the Arabs consider a thing abundant, they judge it to have no end even if it is finite in itself. And it is possible that they are judged to have no end out of consideration for the negative and privative aspects, because they have no end. And as for the meanings...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage combines Ghazali's introduction to the first pillar of faith with an extended commentary by Ibn al-Humam from his work al-Musayara. Ghazali states that the first pillar concerns knowledge of God's essence and His oneness, and it revolves around ten principles. He then gives the first principle: knowledge of God's existence. He adds that the primary source of illumination and reflection is divine guidance. Ibn al-Humam then interjects a clarification: knowledge of God's existence is easy because 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous. He then digresses into the three types of divine attributes: obligatory (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz). Under the obligatory category, he mentions twenty attributes. He discusses whether God's attributes are limited to these twenty, stating that they are dependent on His perfections which are infinite. However, we are only required to believe in what proof indicates, which are these twenty. He explains the infinity of perfections: from our perspective, we cannot fully grasp them; from God's perspective, He knows them as infinite. He also notes that Arabic usage may call something infinite when it is abundant. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the 'meanings' (al-ma'ani), likely continuing into further classification.

معرفة ذات الله
العلم بوجوده
الإلهيات
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God's existence is a difficult philosophical problem that only scholars can grasp.
Correction: Ibn al-Humam states it is easy, because knowledge and cognition are the same linguistically, indicating it is accessible to all.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Humam, how many types of divine matters are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The first step in theology is recognizing that God's attributes are rationally categorized into obligatory, impossible, and possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn al-Humam says knowledge of God's existence is 'easy' (سهل). But when you examine your own heart, do you truly live as if His existence is the most certain and obvious reality? If not, what specific distraction are you allowing to cloud this fundamental truth, and how will you remove it starting today?
?
The passage divides God's attributes into what is obligatory, impossible, and possible. Which of these categories do you find yourself unknowingly violating in your imagination of God? For example, do you ever think of God as limited by time (impossible) or as needing to act in a certain way (denying His possibility)?
?
Knowing that God's actions are 'possible'—He may or may not do them—how does this affect your reliance on Him? When you pray for something, do you truly accept that God may not answer it, yet still trust His wisdom completely, or does your heart cling to a sense of entitlement?

The Twenty Obligatory Attributes and the Role of Proof
Ibn al-Humam defines the twenty obligatory attributes and clarifies the role of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that there are twenty obligatory attributes we must believe in, but God's perfections are infinite; we are only accountable for what has proof.

Core Idea
God's perfections are limitless, but we believe only those attributes that proof establishes.

Having introduced the three categories, Ibn al-Humam now focuses on the first category: what is obligatory for God. He lists twenty attributes but immediately clarifies that these are not exhaustive—God's perfections are infinite. This lesson explains the relationship between obligatory belief and the role of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Focus on the first type
Read the beginning where Ibn al-Humam discusses the first type: what is obligatory for God. Note the mention of twenty attributes.
2
Consider the question of limitation
The passage asks whether attributes are limited to these twenty. Understand the correct answer: they are dependent on perfections which have no end.
The word 'tabi'a' (dependent) indicates that attributes flow from perfections.
3
Grasp the role of proof
The passage states that we are not held accountable for what no rational or transmitted proof indicates. Therefore, the twenty attributes are those for which proof exists.
This protects against speculation.

The Meaning of Infinite Perfections
Ibn al-Humam analyzes the meaning of infinite perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different interpretations of the statement 'His perfections have no end' from the perspectives of human knowledge, divine knowledge, Arabic language, and negative attributes.

Core Idea
The infinity of divine perfections is understood through human limitation, divine omniscience, linguistic usage, and negations.

After establishing that God's perfections are infinite, Ibn al-Humam delves into what this actually means. Does it refer to our inability to comprehend them, God's own knowledge of them, or a linguistic convention? This lesson explores these nuanced interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two perspectives
Read the first part: is 'no end' with regard to our knowledge or God's knowledge? Understand the difference.
2
Consider the linguistic possibility
Read the explanation that Arabic usage can call something abundant 'endless' even if it is finite. This is a linguistic nuance.
3
Reflect on negative attributes
The passage mentions that 'no end' may be judged due to negative and privative aspects (nafsiyya wa salbiyya). Understand that this refers to attributes that are not positive descriptions.
The passage cuts off at 'meanings'.

Divine Attributes

Finite and Infinite Divine Attributes
Ghazali classifies attributes into finite (meaning-based) and infinite (essential and negative).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of divine attributes into finite (meaning-based) and infinite (essential and negative), and the rationale that attributes involving creation are finite.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes are infinite; some are finite because they involve creation.

Ghazali presents a nuanced classification of God's attributes. While many assume all divine attributes are equally infinite, he distinguishes those that relate to creation (which is finite) from those that are purely essential or negative (which are infinite). This passage introduces that distinction and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two categories: 'meaning-based' attributes and 'essential/negative' attributes.
The passage is dense; focus on the contrast between finite and infinite.
2
Understand why meaning-based attributes are finite
The text states: 'because everything that enters into existence is finite.' Meaning-based attributes relate to God's actions in creation, which is finite, so they are considered finite in their instantiation.
3
Reflect on the infinite attributes
Essential and negative attributes do not depend on creation; they are intrinsic to God's essence or deny imperfection, hence they are infinite. The passage combines both finite and infinite attributes and judges all to be without end—possibly meaning the divine essence as a whole is infinite.
This is a subtle point; the text may be ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنوية فهي متناهية، لأن كل ما دخل في الوجود فهو متناه، فتضم ما ينتاهى وهي المعاني والمعنويه إلى ما لا يتناهى وهي النفسية والسلبية، وتحكم على الجميع بعدم النهاية
And the meaning-based (attributes) are finite, because everything that enters into existence is finite. So you combine what is finite, which are the meanings and the meaning-based, with what is infinite, which are the essential and negative (attributes), and you judge all of them to have no end.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes into two categories: those that are finite (mutanahiya) and those that are infinite (ghayr mutanahiya). Ghazali states that 'the meaning-based (al-ma'nawiyya) are finite, because everything that enters into existence is finite.' Here, 'meaning-based attributes' likely refer to attributes that entail a relation to creation or to something that comes into existence (such as will, power, knowledge as they relate to created things). Since creation is finite, these attributes are finite in their instantiation. Then he says: 'So you combine what is finite, which are the meanings and the meaning-based, with what is infinite, which are the essential (al-nafsiyya) and negative (al-salbiyya) (attributes), and you judge all of them to have no end.' This suggests that the essential attributes (those that are identical with God's essence, like existence, self-subsistence) and the negative attributes (those that deny imperfection, like not being a body, not being in a place) are infinite. However, the text may be difficult to parse; the word 'tadummu' (تضم) might mean 'include' or 'combine'. The precise meaning is that the finite meaning-based attributes are included within the infinite essential and negative attributes, and all together are judged to be without end—perhaps meaning that the divine essence as a whole is infinite. This is a nuanced theological point about how God's attributes are conceived in relation to creation.

Key Terms
معنوية
نفسية
سلبية
متناهية
غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all divine attributes are equally infinite.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes attributes that involve creation (finite) from those that are purely negative or essential (infinite).

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why are 'meaning-based' attributes (al-ma'nawiyya) finite?
A) Because they are not real attributes.
B) Because they are not part of God's essence.
C) Because they involve what enters into existence, which is finite.
D) Because they are temporal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes divine attributes based on whether they involve creation (finite) or are purely about God's essence or negation (infinite).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, what aspects of your relationship with God do you treat as finite or limited, as if His mercy and power depend on your actions? How does Ghazali's distinction between finite and infinite attributes challenge you to expand your trust in God's infinite essence?
?
Ghazali says meaning-based attributes are finite because they involve creation. Reflect on a specific attribute of God you call upon, like Ar-Razzaq (The Provider). Do you believe His provision is finite because your circumstances seem limited, or do you recognize that His essential provision is infinite? What would change if you lived today with the certainty that His infinite attribute encompasses your finitude?
?
If God's essential and negative attributes are infinite, what does that imply about the value and permanence of your own essence? How does the reality that you are a finite creation, dependent on an infinite Creator, reshape your priorities and the way you seek nearness to Him?

The Second Principle: God's Eternity
Ghazali defines God's eternity and proves it via impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of God's eternity and the proof from the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's eternity is proven by the absurdity of an endless chain of causes.

Imam Ghazali now turns to the second attribute necessary for God: eternity. He defines what it means for God to be eternal and then gives a logical proof that God cannot have a beginning. This proof is a classic argument in Islamic theology that cuts off any possibility of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through.
Notice that Ghazali uses the term 'qadim' for eternal.
2
Identify the definition of eternity
Find where Ghazali defines what it means for God to be eternal. Note the phrases 'lam yazal', 'azali', and 'la awwala li wujudihi'.
Eternity here means no beginning, prior to all things.
3
Follow the proof step by step
Trace the logical conditional: if originated then needs an originator, leading to infinite regress. Understand why the regress is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
Ghazali states 'what regresses cannot obtain'. Reflect on why an actual infinite chain of causes cannot exist.
The argument assumes that every effect requires a cause, and the chain must terminate.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider that this proof establishes God as the first cause, eternal without beginning. Appreciate how the argument is used in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني القدم. العلم بأن الله تعالى قديم لم يزل، أزلي ليس لوجوده أول بل أول كل شيء وقبل كل شيء. وبرهانه أنه لو كان حادثاً ولم يكن قديماً لافتقر هو أيضاً إلى محدث، وافتقر محدثه إلى محدث، وتسلسل ذلك إلى ما لا نهاية، وما تسلسل لم يتحصل.
The second principle: Eternity. Knowledge that God the Exalted is eternal, has not ceased to be, pre-eternal without beginning for His existence, rather He is the first of everything and before everything. Its proof: that if He were originated and not eternal, He would also need an originator, and His originator would need another originator, and that would regress endlessly, and what regresses cannot obtain [i.e., is impossible].
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This is a definition and proof passage. Imam Ghazali states the second attribute of God, which is eternity (al-qidam). He defines it as God having no beginning and being pre-existent. Then he provides a proof: if God were not eternal, He would be originated and would require an originator, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal. This proof establishes that God's existence is without beginning.

Key Terms
قدم
أزلي
حادث
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is a positive attribute added to God's essence.
Correction: Eternity (qidam) is a negative attribute meaning absence of prior non-existence, not an added quality.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second principle, what proves that God is eternal?
A) He is the creator of the world.
B) He is all-powerful.
C) If He were originated, He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress.
D) He is one.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity is logically necessary because an infinite chain of causes cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has no beginning, then every moment of your life is contingent on Him. What does that mean for the way you view your own plans and achievements? Are you living as though you are the origin of your own success, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on the Eternal?
?
The proof shows that an infinite regress of causes cannot exist. In your own search for meaning and security, where do you ultimately anchor yourself? Do you rely on temporary things that themselves need causes, or have you truly placed your trust in the Eternal One who needs no cause?
?
If you truly believed that God's eternity means He existed before anything else and will remain after everything, how would that change your priorities today? What would you stop worrying about, and what would you start seeking more earnestly?

The Definition and Proof of Allah's Pre-eternity
Ghazali defines pre-eternity and proves it by infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what pre-eternity (qidam) means when attributed to Allah and the logical proof that establishes it through the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Allah's pre-eternity means His existence has no beginning; the proof avoids infinite regress.

Imam al-Ghazali begins the second principle of creed by defining the pre-eternity of Allah. He provides a rational argument that if Allah were originated, He would need an originator, leading to an impossible infinite chain. This lesson lays out that definition and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of pre-eternity: Allah is pre-eternal, has no beginning, and is the first of everything. Notice the terms qadim and azali.
The definition is concise; make sure you understand each phrase.
2
Study the proof structure
The proof uses a conditional: if Allah were originated, He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Recognise this as a reductio ad absurdum.
Infinite regress is considered impossible in Islamic theology.
3
Identify the conclusion
The argument concludes that the regress must end with a pre-eternal originator, whom we call the Creator. This is the desired outcome.
The proof establishes the necessity of a pre-eternal being.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why infinite regress is impossible: it would prevent any existence from occurring. Then ponder how this proof establishes tawhid.
This proof is a foundation for many other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني القدم
العلم بأن الله تعالى قديم لم يزل، أزلي ليس لوجوده أول بل أول كل شيء وقبل كل شيء.
وبرهانه أنه لو كان حادثاً ولم يكن قديماً لافتقر هو أيضاً إلى محدث، وافتقر محدثه إلى محدث، وتسلسل ذلك إلى ما لا نهاية، وما تسلسل لم يتحصل، أو ينتهي إلى محدث قديم هو الأول، وذلك هو المطلوب الذي سميناه صانع العالم ومبدئه وبارئه ومحدثه ومبدعه.
وقال إمام الحرمين: حدوث الجواهر بني على أصول: منها إثبات الأعراض، ومنها إثبات حدوثها، ومنها استحالة تعري الجواهر منها، ومنها إثبات استحالة حوادث لا أول لها، ومنها أن ما لا يسبق الحوادث حادث.
ثم القدم هي صفة سلبية على الأصح، فهي عبارة عن سلب العدم السابق على الوجود، هذا معنى القدم في حقه تعالى وفي حق صفاته؛ ومعنى أزلي: نسبة إلى الأزل وهو القديم كما في الصحاح والتهذيب، فهو حينئذ بمعنى القديم.
والمراد: أنه سبحانه لم يسبق وجوده عدم، يعني أن القدم في حقه تعالى بمعنى الأزلية التي هي كون وجوده غير مستفتح.
وللإمام الغزالي في الاقتصاد تعليق نفيس يقول فيه: ليس تحت لفظ القديم يعني في حق الله تعالى سوى إثبات موجود، ونفي عدم سابق، فلا تظنن أن القدم معنى زائد على ذات القديم فيلزمك أن تقول ذلك المعنى أيضا قديم بقدم زائد عليه، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية.
وبيانه أنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً لوجوب انحصار كل موجود في القدم والحدوث فمهما انتفى أحدهما تعين الآخر، والحدوث على الله عز وجل مستحيل، لأنه يستلزم له محدث، لأن لكل حادث لا بد له من محدث فينقل الكلام إلى ذلك المحدث فإن كان قديماً فهو المراد بمسمى كلمة الجلالة، وإن لم يكن قديماً كان حادثاً، وينتهي إلى محدث قديم هو الأول، وذلك هو المطلوب.
(وفي نسخة أخرى: ومبدئه)
The Second Principle: Pre-eternity.
Knowledge that Allah the Exalted is pre-eternal, has not ceased, eternal, there is no beginning to His existence, rather He is the first of everything and before everything.
Its proof: if He were originated and not pre-eternal, then He would also need an originator, and His originator would need an originator, and that would chain infinitely, and what chains does not obtain, or it ends at an originator who is pre-eternal and is the First, and that is what is sought, which we have called the Maker of the world, its Initiator, its Creator, its Producer, and its Inventor.
Imam al-Haramayn said: the origination of atoms is built upon principles: establishing accidents, establishing their origination, the impossibility of atoms being free of them, establishing the impossibility of events with no beginning, and that what does not precede events is originated.
Then pre-eternity is a negative attribute according to the soundest view, for it is the negation of non-existence preceding existence; this is the meaning of pre-eternity with respect to Him and with respect to His attributes. The meaning of 'eternal' (azali) is attribution to al-azal which is the pre-eternal, as in al-Sihah and al-Tahdhib, so it is synonymous with pre-eternal.
And the intent: that His existence is not preceded by non-existence, meaning that pre-eternity with respect to Him is in the sense of eternalness (azaliyya), which is that His existence is not begun.
Imam al-Ghazali has a fine comment in al-Iqtisad in which he says: there is nothing under the term 'pre-eternal'—meaning with respect to Allah—except affirming an existent and negating prior non-existence. So do not think that pre-eternity is an additional meaning beyond the essence of the Pre-eternal, lest you be forced to say that that meaning is also pre-eternal with an additional pre-eternity, and so chain ad infinitum.
And its explanation: if He were not pre-eternal, then He would be originated because every existent is necessarily confined to pre-eternity and origination; whenever one is negated the other is affirmed. Origination is impossible for Allah, because it entails an originator for Him, since every originated thing necessarily has an originator. Then the discourse is transferred to that originator: if He is pre-eternal, then He is what is meant by the word of majesty; if He is not pre-eternal, then He is originated, and it ends at a pre-eternal originator who is the First, and that is what is sought.
(And in another copy: 'its Initiator').
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the second principle: the pre-eternity (qidam) of Allah. It begins by stating the definition: Allah is pre-eternal, with no beginning to His existence, and He is the first of everything. Then it provides the proof: if He were originated, He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible; therefore the chain must end with a pre-eternal originator, who is Allah. The passage then includes a note from Imam al-Haramayn on the principles of the origination of atoms, which underpin the proof. Next, it clarifies that pre-eternity is a negative attribute—it merely negates prior non-existence—and warns against considering it an additional attribute, quoting al-Ghazali's Iqtisad. The proof is further elaborated by arguing that if Allah were not pre-eternal, He would be originated, and the impossibility of that shows He must be pre-eternal.

Key Terms
قدم
قديم
أزلي
حادث
حدوث
تسلسل
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity means Allah is very old or has existed for a long time.
Correction: Pre-eternity means His existence has no beginning at all; it is not a measure of age but the absence of a starting point.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if we assume Allah is originated (hadith)?
A) He would still be pre-eternal
B) He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress
C) He would be independent of any originator
D) The proof would be invalid

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity of Allah is proven by the impossibility of infinite regress; it means His existence has no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah has no beginning, how would that change the way you think about your own beginning and your complete dependence on Him? Will you start each day acknowledging that He is the First and you are utterly contingent?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible and there must be a Pre-eternal Originator. Are there areas of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every good thing comes from the One who has no beginning? What is one habit you can change today to live in that dependency?
?
When you plan your future, do you operate with the illusion that your existence is self-starting, or do you realize that you exist only because of the Eternal One? How can you remind yourself daily that He is 'before everything' and that your life is entirely in His hands?

Pre-eternity as a Negative Attribute
Ghazali defines pre-eternity as a negative attribute and warns against misunderstanding it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that pre-eternity (qidam) is a negative attribute, meaning the negation of prior non-existence, and why it cannot be considered a separate positive attribute.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is not a positive attribute; it simply negates any non-existence before Allah.

After establishing the proof of pre-eternity, Imam al-Ghazali clarifies the precise meaning of the term. He explains that pre-eternity is a negative attribute—it only denies that Allah ever did not exist. He warns against thinking it is a separate quality, which would lead to an infinite regress of pre-eternities. This lesson unpacks that clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of negative attribute
The passage states that pre-eternity is a negative attribute: it is the negation of non-existence preceding existence. Understand this carefully.
Negative attributes are those that deny imperfections.
2
Understand the synonym 'azali'
The term 'azali' is synonymous with 'qadim', both meaning pre-eternal. It is derived from 'al-azal' which means pre-eternity.
Lexicographers confirm this equivalence.
3
Study Ghazali's warning
Ghazali warns not to think pre-eternity is an extra meaning added to Allah's essence, because that would lead to an infinite regress of pre-eternities. This is a key theological point.
This warning preserves tawhid by avoiding multiplicity in the Divine.
4
Reflect on the proof restatement
The passage ends with a restatement of the proof: if not pre-eternal, then originated, which is impossible for Allah. This reaffirms the necessity of pre-eternity.
This ties back to the earlier lesson.

Source Text

The Third Principle: The Permanence of Allah
Ghazali defines permanence and proves it by the impossibility of self-annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's permanence and the logical proof that His existence cannot cease.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is necessary, so it cannot be annihilated.

This lesson introduces the third theological principle: the permanence (baqa') of Allah. Imam Ghazali establishes that Allah, being pre-eternal, cannot undergo annihilation. He provides a logical proof based on the impossibility of self-annihilation or external annihilation, and clarifies the precise meaning of permanence, distinguishing it from temporal endurance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, proof, and commentary.
The passage includes both the main text and commentary in parentheses.
2
Understand the definition of permanence
Permanence is defined as the negation of non-existence after existence. For Allah, it is absolute impossibility of ceasing to exist.
The passage also mentions a secondary temporal meaning that does not apply to Allah.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof considers two possibilities for annihilation: by itself or by an external annihilator. Both are shown to be impossible.
Self-annihilation would imply self-existence, which is impossible. External annihilation requires a pre-eternal annihilator, which cannot be.
4
Consider the alternative proof from Ibn al-Hammam
Since Allah's existence is necessary from His own essence, His essence cannot cause non-existence. Hence, He must be permanent.
5
Reflect on the distinctive meaning
Permanence for Allah is not temporal duration but the impossibility of non-existence. This corrects a common misunderstanding.
The Qur'anic names 'First' and 'Last' point to eternality and everlastingness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث البقاء
العلم بأنه تعالى مع كونه أزلياً أبدياً ليس لوجوده آخر، فهو الأول والآخر، والظاهر والباطن، لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه.
وبرهانه: أنه لو انعدم لكان لا يخلو إما أن ينعدم بنفسه أو بمعدم يضاده، ولو جاز أن ينعدم شيء يتصور دوامه بنفسه لجاز أن يوجد شيء يتصور عدمه بنفسه فكما يحتاج طريان الوجود إلى سبب فكذلك يحتاج طريان العدم إلى سبب، فينعدم بمعدم يضاده لأن ذلك المعدم لو كان قديماً لما تصور عدمه، وباطل أن يكون عدمه بنفسه.
(ومعنى البقاء في حقه تعالى: هو سلب العدم اللاحق للوجود، ويطلق البقاء بمعنى مقارنة الوجود لزمانين فصاعداً، وهو محال في حقه تعالى لاستحالة تقييد وجوده بالزمان. وفي تفسير قوله 'فهو الأول والآخر والظاهر والباطن': الأول دليل أزليته، والآخر دليل أبديته، والظاهر والباطن وارد في كتابه العزيز. وقوله 'لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه' مبني على المشهور من أن القديم أخص من الأزلي. وقرره ابن الهمام بوجه آخر فقال: لأنه لما ثبت أنه الموجد الذي استندت إليه كل الموجودات، ثبت عدم استناد وجوده إلى غيره فيلزم أن يكون وجوده له من نفسه، أي اقتضت ذاته المقدسة اقتضاء تاماً، فإذا ثبت أن وجوده مقتضى ذاته المقدسة استحال أن تؤثر ذاته عدمها لأن ما بالذات يعني ما تقتضيه الذات اقتضاء تاماً لا يتخلف عنها. وقد تختصر العبارة عن ذلك فيقال لأنه واجب الوجوب لا يقبل الانتفاء بحال فيلزم بقاؤه.)
The Third Principle: Permanence.
Knowledge that He the Exalted, while being eternal and everlasting, has no end to His existence, so He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden, because what is established as pre-eternal, its non-existence is impossible.
Its proof: if He were to cease, it would be either by Himself or by an annihilator that opposes Him. If it were possible for something whose continuation is conceivable in itself to cease, then it would be possible for something whose non-existence is conceivable in itself to exist. So just as the advent of existence needs a cause, likewise the advent of non-existence needs a cause; thus He would cease by an annihilator that opposes Him, because that annihilator, if it were pre-eternal, its non-existence would not be conceivable. And it is false that His non-existence is by Himself.
(The meaning of permanence with respect to Him is the negation of non-existence subsequent to existence; permanence is also applied to mean the co-existence of existence with two or more times, but this is impossible with respect to Him due to the impossibility of restricting His existence by time. In the interpretation of His saying 'He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden': 'the First' is proof of His eternality, 'the Last' is proof of His everlastingness, and 'the Manifest and the Hidden' occur in His noble Book. His saying 'because what is established as pre-eternal, its non-existence is impossible' is built upon the well-known view that the pre-eternal is more specific than the eternal. Ibn al-Hammam explained it in another way: because when it is established that He is the Creator to whom all existents are dependent, it is established that His existence is not dependent on another, so it must be that His existence is from Himself, i.e., His Holy Essence is a complete requirement; then if it is established that His existence is required by His Holy Essence, it is impossible for His Essence to effect its own non-existence, because that which is essential, meaning what the Essence requires completely, does not fail. This is summarised by saying: He is the Necessary Existent, which does not admit non-existence in any case, so His permanence necessarily follows.)
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the third principle: the permanence (baqa') of Allah. It begins with the definition: Allah is eternal and everlasting, with no end to His existence; He is the First and the Last, etc., because what is pre-eternal cannot cease. The proof then considers the hypothetical annihilation of Allah: if He were to cease, the annihilation would be either self-caused or caused by an external annihilator. Self-annihilation is argued to be impossible, as it would imply that existence also could be self-caused, contradicting the need for a cause. External annihilation is also impossible because the annihilator would have to be pre-eternal, but nothing pre-eternal can be conceived to be non-existent. Thus, annihilation is impossible. The passage then provides commentary: permanence is a negative attribute—negation of subsequent non-existence; a secondary meaning of permanence (co-existence with multiple times) does not apply to Allah. It interprets the Qur'anic names and explains that the proof is based on the pre-eternal being more specific than the eternal. Finally, Ibn al-Hammam offers an alternative proof: since Allah's existence is necessary from His own essence, His essence cannot cause non-existence, so He necessarily remains.

Key Terms

واجب الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Permanence means enduring through time.
Correction: For Allah, permanence is the negation of non-existence after existence, not temporal duration.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'permanence' (baqa') with respect to Allah?
A) Endless duration in time
B) The negation of non-existence after existence
C) The attribute of everlasting life
D) That He is the First and the Last

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is necessary from His essence, so He cannot cease to exist; His permanence is the absolute impossibility of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence is necessary and permanent, why do you still fear the future as if He might fail you? What would change if you truly internalized that He cannot cease, while everything else does?
?
Your own existence is contingent and temporary. Knowing that Allah alone is permanent, what have you placed your ultimate hope in that will not last?
?
The proof shows that self-annihilation is impossible for Allah. Yet you sometimes act as if your own efforts are self-sustaining. Where in your life do you need to recognize that you are not the source of your own existence?

God's Permanence Defined
Ghazali defines God's permanence and links it to His pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's permanence means His existence has no end, and that it follows necessarily from His pre-eternity.

Core Idea
God's permanence is simply the corollary of His pre-eternity.

In this lesson, Ghazali establishes the third principle of faith: God's permanence (al-baqa'). He begins by stating that God is both eternal and everlasting, with no termination of His existence. This sets the foundation for the rational proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the key attributes mentioned.
2
Identify the key attributes
List the attributes: eternal (azali), everlasting (abadi), First, Last, Outward, Inward. Understand how they relate to permanence.
3
Understand the logical link
Focus on the clause 'because whatever is proven to have pre-eternity, its non-existence is impossible'. This is the core logical connection.
4
Reflect on the meaning of permanence
Consider that permanence is not a separate positive attribute but the necessary continuation of existence. This will be argued in the proof.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم بأنه تعالى مع كونه أزلياً أبدياً ليس لوجوده آخر، فهو الأول والآخر، والظاهر والباطن، لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه. وبرهانه: أنه لو انعدم لكان لا يخلو إما أن ينعدم بنفسه أو بمعدم يضاده، ولو جاز أن ينعدم شيء يتصور دوامه بنفسه لجاز أن يوجد شيء يتصور عدمه بنفسه فكما يحتاج طريان الوجود إلى سبب فكذلك يحتاج طريان العدم إلى سبب. وباطل أن ينعدم بمعدم يضاده لأن ذلك المعدم لو كان قديماً لما تصور الوجود معه، وقد ظهر بالأصلين السابقين وجوده وقدمه، فكيف كان وجوده في القدم ومعه ضده؟ فإن كان الضد المعدم حادثاً كان محالاً إذ ليس الحادث في مضادته للقديم حتى يقطع وجوده بأولى من القديم في مضادته للحادث حتى يدفع وجوده، بل الدفع أهون من القطع، والقديم أقوى وأولى من الحادث.
Knowledge that He, exalted, although being eternal and everlasting, has no end to His existence. So He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward, because whatever is proven to have pre-eternity, its non-existence is impossible. Its proof: if it were to become non-existent, it would be either that it becomes non-existent by itself or by a contrary that annihilates it. If it were permissible for something whose duration is conceivable by itself to become non-existent, then it would be permissible for something whose non-existence is conceivable by itself to exist. So just as the occurrence of existence requires a cause, so too the occurrence of non-existence requires a cause. It is false that it becomes non-existent by a contrary that annihilates it, because if that contrary were eternal, then existence would not be conceivable with it. And it has become clear from the two preceding principles His existence and His pre-eternity. So how could His existence be in pre-eternity while with Him is a contrary? For if the annihilating contrary were originated, it would be impossible, for the originated is not such in its opposition to the eternal that it would cut off its existence rather than the eternal in its opposition to the originated would repel its existence. Rather, repelling is easier than cutting off, and the eternal is stronger and more primordial than the originated.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the third principle of God's permanence (al-baqa') and provides a rational proof. It begins by stating that God, being eternal and everlasting, has no end to His existence. The proof then argues that if God were to cease to exist, it would have to be either by Himself or by an external contrary. The first case is impossible because if something that exists essentially could become non-existent by itself, then the opposite would also be possible: something non-existent could come to exist by itself, which is false. So non-existence, like existence, requires a cause. The second case is refuted by considering the nature of the supposed contrary. If the contrary were eternal, it would have prevented God's existence from the beginning, but God already exists. If the contrary were originated, then a weak originated being cannot overpower an eternal being. The argument concludes that God's permanence is necessary.

Key Terms
بقاء
أزلي
أبدي
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's permanence is a separate positive attribute.
Correction: In fact, permanence is simply the necessary continuation of His existence, implied by His pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is God's non-existence impossible?
A) Because He is the First and Last.
B) Because He is eternal and everlasting.
C) Because whatever is proven to have pre-eternity cannot become non-existent.
D) Because He is Outward and Inward.

Key Takeaway
God's permanence is not an additional attribute but the necessary continuation of His existence, grounded in His pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God alone is permanent and everything else will perish, where is your true hope anchored? Is your heart clinging to something that will end, or to the One who never ends?
?
Knowing that your existence is temporary while His is eternal, what does that demand from you today? What one concrete action can you take right now that will have lasting value in light of His permanence?
?
When you face loss, pain, or uncertainty, do you truly live as though God remains? How can you train yourself to turn to Him first, remembering that He is the Last who never leaves?

Proof of God's Permanence
Ghazali provides a rational proof for God's necessary permanence by refuting both self-annihilation and annihilation by a contrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rational proof that God cannot cease to exist, refuting both self-annihilation and annihilation by a contrary.

Core Idea
Nothing that exists essentially can become non-existent without a cause; and no cause can overpower the eternal.

Having defined God's permanence, Ghazali now provides a rigorous proof. He considers the only two possible ways cessation of existence could occur and demonstrates both are impossible. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The proof is dense and uses logical reasoning.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The proof begins by stating that if God were to become non-existent, it would be either by itself or by a contrary. Note these two branches.
3
Analyze the first refutation
The first possibility (self-annihilation) is refuted because if something whose duration is conceivable by itself could cease to exist, then the opposite would also be possible: something non-existent could come to exist by itself. This is false, so non-existence requires a cause.
4
Analyze the second refutation
The second possibility (annihilation by a contrary) is refuted by considering the nature of the contrary. If the contrary were eternal, it would have prevented God's existence from the start. If it were originated, it cannot overpower the eternal.
The phrase 'repelling is easier than cutting off' is key.
5
Understand the conclusion
Both possibilities are impossible, therefore God's permanence is necessary. The proof relies on the previous principles of existence and pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم بأنه تعالى مع كونه أزلياً أبدياً ليس لوجوده آخر، فهو الأول والآخر، والظاهر والباطن، لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه. وبرهانه: أنه لو انعدم لكان لا يخلو إما أن ينعدم بنفسه أو بمعدم يضاده، ولو جاز أن ينعدم شيء يتصور دوامه بنفسه لجاز أن يوجد شيء يتصور عدمه بنفسه فكما يحتاج طريان الوجود إلى سبب فكذلك يحتاج طريان العدم إلى سبب. وباطل أن ينعدم بمعدم يضاده لأن ذلك المعدم لو كان قديماً لما تصور الوجود معه، وقد ظهر بالأصلين السابقين وجوده وقدمه، فكيف كان وجوده في القدم ومعه ضده؟ فإن كان الضد المعدم حادثاً كان محالاً إذ ليس الحادث في مضادته للقديم حتى يقطع وجوده بأولى من القديم في مضادته للحادث حتى يدفع وجوده، بل الدفع أهون من القطع، والقديم أقوى وأولى من الحادث.
Knowledge that He, exalted, although being eternal and everlasting, has no end to His existence. So He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward, because whatever is proven to have pre-eternity, its non-existence is impossible. Its proof: if it were to become non-existent, it would be either that it becomes non-existent by itself or by a contrary that annihilates it. If it were permissible for something whose duration is conceivable by itself to become non-existent, then it would be permissible for something whose non-existence is conceivable by itself to exist. So just as the occurrence of existence requires a cause, so too the occurrence of non-existence requires a cause. It is false that it becomes non-existent by a contrary that annihilates it, because if that contrary were eternal, then existence would not be conceivable with it. And it has become clear from the two preceding principles His existence and His pre-eternity. So how could His existence be in pre-eternity while with Him is a contrary? For if the annihilating contrary were originated, it would be impossible, for the originated is not such in its opposition to the eternal that it would cut off its existence rather than the eternal in its opposition to the originated would repel its existence. Rather, repelling is easier than cutting off, and the eternal is stronger and more primordial than the originated.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the third principle of God's permanence (al-baqa') and provides a rational proof. It begins by stating that God, being eternal and everlasting, has no end to His existence. The proof then argues that if God were to cease to exist, it would have to be either by Himself or by an external contrary. The first case is impossible because if something that exists essentially could become non-existent by itself, then the opposite would also be possible: something non-existent could come to exist by itself, which is false. So non-existence, like existence, requires a cause. The second case is refuted by considering the nature of the supposed contrary. If the contrary were eternal, it would have prevented God's existence from the beginning, but God already exists. If the contrary were originated, then a weak originated being cannot overpower an eternal being. The argument concludes that God's permanence is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why can an originated contrary not annihilate the eternal God?
A) Because an originated being cannot exist alongside the eternal.
B) Because the eternal is stronger and repelling is easier than cutting off.
C) Because an originated contrary would have prevented existence from the start.
D) Because non-existence requires a cause.

Key Takeaway
God's permanence is rationally necessary because neither self-annihilation nor annihilation by an external cause is possible for the eternal being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Knowing that God's existence is necessary and not subject to change, what does that mean for your hope in His mercy and your fear of His punishment? Are you living as if He is truly permanent and all else is transient?
?

Summary of Divine Attributes and Transcendence
Ghazali summarizes the attributes derived from previous principles, affirming God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali summarizes the attributes of God and affirms His absolute transcendence from creation.

Core Idea
God's attributes prove His transcendence — nothing resembles Him.

Imam al-Ghazali now brings together the principles established earlier to present a summary of what we can affirm about God and what we must deny. This passage is both a conclusion and a declaration: God is not like anything in creation, and the very attributes that prove His existence also prove His utter uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from attributes to the rejection of similarity.
The passage uses a series of short statements building to a strong rhetorical conclusion.
2
Identify the attributes and their implication
Find the four attributes mentioned at the beginning. Understand why they cannot apply to accidents and what they imply about God's nature.
The attributes are Knowledge, Power, Will, and Creativity.
3
Understand the conclusion of transcendence
Notice how the passage moves from attributes to the statement that God is not a substance or accident, and then to the absolute denial of resemblance. Reflect on the rhetorical question at the end.
The phrase 'ليس كمثله شيء' is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه عالم قادر مريد خالق (٢)، وهذه الأوصاف تستحيل على الأعراض بل لا تعقل إلا لموجود (٣) قائم بنفسه، مستقل بذاته، وقد تحصل من هذه الأصول أنه موجود (٤) قائم بنفسه، ليس بجوهر ولا عرض (٥)، وأن العالم كله جواهر وأعراض وأجسام (٦) فإذا لا يشبه شيئا ولا يشبهه شيء (٧) بل الحي القيوم (٨) الذي ليس كمثله شيء (٩)، وأنى يشبه المخلوق خالقه، والمقدور مقدره، والمصور مصوره، والأجسام والأعراض كلها من خلقه وصنعه فاستحال القضاء عليها بمماثلته ومشابهته.
And because He is Knowing, Powerful, Willing, Creator (2), and these attributes are impossible for accidents, rather they are only conceivable for a self-subsistent existent (3), independent in essence. And from these principles it is concluded that He is an existent (4), self-subsistent, not a substance nor an accident (5), and that the entire world is composed of atoms, accidents, and bodies (6). Thus He does not resemble anything nor does anything resemble Him (7), rather He is the Living, the Self-Subsisting (8), the One like whom there is nothing (9). And how could the created resemble its Creator, and the determined resemble its Determiner, and the formed resemble its Formator? The bodies and accidents are all from His creation and making, so it is impossible to judge them as being similar or likened to Him.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the divine attributes and the conclusion of God's transcendence. It begins by stating that God is described as Knowing, Powerful, Willing, and Creator. These attributes cannot apply to accidents, which are temporary and dependent; they only befit a being that is self-subsistent and independent in essence. The author then says that from these principles it is established that God is an existent who is self-subsistent, not a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad). The world is composed of atoms (jawahir), accidents (a'rad), and bodies (ajsam). Since God is the Creator of all these, He does not resemble any of them, nor do they resemble Him. He is al-Hayy al-Qayyum, the Living and Self-Subsisting, and nothing is like Him. The rhetorical question 'How could the created resemble its Creator?' reinforces the impossibility of similarity. The passage concludes that since bodies and accidents are all His creation and making, it is impossible to judge them as being similar or equal to Him.

الحي القيوم
كمثله شيء
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that describing God as 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' implies He has a life similar to created life.
Correction: The passage immediately follows with 'ليس كمثله شيء' and emphasizes total dissimilarity, so God's life is unlike any created life.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can the attributes of knowledge, power, and will not apply to accidents?
A) Because accidents are not created by God
B) Because accidents are temporary and dependent, while these attributes require a self-subsistent being
C) Because accidents are substances
D) Because accidents are eternal

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of knowledge, power, will, and creativity prove that He is self-subsistent and absolutely unlike His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim God is unlike anything, but do you still imagine Him in your mind as a being with form or location? How can you worship a God you cannot picture? What must change in your prayer to truly recognize that nothing resembles Him?
?
If you truly believe that nothing is like Him, then why do you fear creation more than you fear Him? Why do you seek comfort from created things rather than from the One who created them? Will you commit to turning your heart solely to Him today?
?
The passage asks: 'How could the created resemble its Creator?' But in your daily life, do you treat God's attributes as if they were like human attributes? When you say 'God is Knowing,' do you think of His knowledge as limited like yours? Reflect on one area where you have unconsciously likened God to His creation, and resolve to correct that thought starting today.
