Interpreting Istiwa': Affirming Divine Transcendence
Ghazali details the orthodox interpretation of istiwa' as istila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the orthodox response to the verse of istiwa' affirms God's transcendence while accepting the literal text's meaning through interpretation (ta'wil).

Core Idea
Istiwa' is affirmed, but not like bodily settling—it means divine domination.

After establishing the impossibility of corporeality for God, scholars faced the challenge of verses that seemingly describe physical actions. This passage presents the detailed answer to the famous verse of istiwa', showing how the orthodox school reconciles the apparent meaning with definitive proofs of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once, noting the key terms. Let the author's own flow guide you before analysis.
Pay attention to the negation and positive affirmation.
2
Identify the key negation
The author immediately negates tamakkun (settling), mumassa (touching), and muhadha (adjacency). Understand why these are impossible for God.
These negations are based on definitive proofs (barahin qat'iyya).
3
Find the positive interpretation
The meaning of istiwa' is given as istila' (domination). Notice the poetic evidence cited to show this linguistic usage.
The poetry is a linguistic argument, not a theological proof.
4
Notice the analogy with other texts
The same method is applied to the verse 'He is with you' and the hadith about the fingers. See how a figurative interpretation is necessary to avoid anthropomorphism.
The people of falsehood (Ahl al-Batil) are also forced to interpret, but wrongly.
5
Reflect on the rationale
Why is ta'wil necessary? Because definitive proofs of transcendence prevent literal readings. This protects tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأجوبة التفصيلية فقد أجيب عن آية الإستواء بأنا نؤمن بأنه تعالى إستوى على العرش مع الحكم بأنه ليس كإستواء الأجسام على الأجسام من التمكن والمماسة والمحاذاة لها لقيام البراهين القطعية بإستحالة ذلك في حقه تعالى ، بل نؤمن بأن الإستواء ثابت له تعالى بمعنى يليق به تعالى والإستيلاء ) كما قال الشاعر : قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق واضطر أهل الحق إلى هذا التأويل كما اضطر أهل الباطل الى تأويل قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَمَا كُنتُم ) إذ حمل ذلك بالإتفاق على الإحاطة والعلم ، وحمل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : قلب المؤمن بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن على القدرة
As for the detailed answers, the verse of istiwa' was answered by: We believe that He, exalted, istawa over the throne, while judging that it is not like the istiwa' of bodies over bodies, of settling, touching, and being adjacent to them, because of the definitive proofs establishing the impossibility of that in His right, exalted. Rather, we believe that istiwa' is affirmed for Him, exalted, in a meaning befitting Him, and (that is) istila' (domination), as the poet said: 'Bishr has istawa over Iraq without sword or spilled blood.' And the people of truth were forced to this interpretation just as the people of falsehood were forced to interpret His statement, exalted: 'And He is with you wherever you are' - as they interpreted it unanimously as encompassing and knowledge. And the Prophet's saying, peace be upon him: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Merciful' was interpreted as (meaning) power.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed answer to the verse of istiwa' (Qur'an 20:5, etc.). It begins by affirming that God istawa over the throne, but immediately negates any similarity to physical beings by specifying that it is not like the settling (tamakkun), touching (mumassa), or adjacency (muhadha) of bodies. Definitive proofs (barahin qat'iyya) establish the impossibility of corporeal attributes for God. Instead, istiwa' is affirmed in a meaning befitting His majesty, and that meaning is given as istila' (domination, overpowering). A line of poetry is cited as linguistic evidence that istiwa' can mean domination without physical contact. The author then notes that the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq, the orthodox) were compelled to this interpretation in the same way that the people of falsehood (Ahl al-Batil) were forced to interpret the verse 'He is with you' (Qur'an 57:4) as knowledge and encompassing, avoiding a literal spatial meaning. Similarly, the hadith about the heart between the fingers of the Merciful is interpreted as 'power' (qudra) to avoid the implication of physical digits.

Key Terms
استوى
إستواء
إستيلاء
تمكن
مماسة
محاذاة
قهر
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpreting istiwa' as istila' is the only valid interpretation.
Correction: The text says istiwa' is affirmed in a meaning befitting Him, and istila' is one possible interpretation, not the exclusive one. The core is affirming divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for interpreting istiwa' as istila'?
A) Reason alone.
B) Poetry and linguistic usage.
C) Consensus of the Salaf.
D) Direct revelation.

Key Takeaway
The orthodox approach to ambiguous verses is to affirm the word's meaning in a manner befitting God's majesty, guided by definitive proofs of transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'The Most Merciful ascended the Throne,' does your heart immediately imagine a physical settling? If so, what does that tell you about how deeply you've internalized Allah's transcendence, and what step will you take today to correct that mental image in your worship?
?
The passage says the people of truth were 'forced' to this interpretation (idtirar). Do you feel any internal resistance when you accept that Allah is not like His creation? What aspect of your own desire to 'visualize' God needs to be surrendered, and how will you train your heart to affirm without likeness?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's istiwa' is domination without physical contact, how would that change your sense of His absolute control over your life? What part of your life are you still acting as if His 'hand' is not in direct control, and what would it take to trust that domination today?

The First Proof from Musa's Request: Prophetic Knowledge
First proof: prophetic request implies permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali uses Musa's request for vision as evidence that vision of Allah is permissible, based on the principle that prophets are knowledgeable of what is possible for Allah.

Core Idea
A prophet would not ask for the impossible.

Al-Ghazali begins his refutation of the Mu'tazilites by examining the story of Prophet Musa (Moses) in the Qur'an. Musa asked Allah to see Him, and Allah replied, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain...' Al-Ghazali argues that if vision were absolutely impossible, a wise prophet like Musa would never have asked for it. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage about the first proof once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
Notice the rhetorical challenge to the Mu'tazilites.
2
Grasp the entailment logic
Follow the logic: Musa is a prophet → he knows what is possible for Allah → if vision were impossible, he wouldn't ask it → but he did ask → therefore vision is possible.
The key premise is that prophets cannot be ignorant of Allah's attributes.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this proof imply about those who deny the vision? It implies they claim to know better than a prophet.
Al-Ghazali uses this as a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقوله تعالى في خطاب موسى عليه السلام : ﴿ لَنْ تَرَانِي ﴾ وليت شعري كيف عرف المعتزلي من صفات رب الأرباب ما جهله موسى عليه السلام ؟(۲) وكيف سأل موسی عليه السلام الرؤية مع كونها محالا ولعل الجهل يذوي البدع والأهواء من الجهلة الأغبياء أولى من الجهل بالأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم (۳) (۱) من الآية (١٤٣) من سورة الأعراف (۷) وتمامها : ﴿ وَلَكِنِ انْظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَتَرَانِي ) ووجه الإستدلال من وجهين : أحدهما : أنه لو لم تجز الرؤية لما طلبها موسى عليه السلام ، واللازم باطل بالإجماع، وتواتر الأخبار بيان اللزوم أن موسى عليه السلام عالم بما يجوز على الله تعالى ، وما يستحيل عليه ، وإلا يلزم الجهل وهو محال على الأنبياء ، وإذا كان عالماً بما لا يجوز الرؤية مما لا يجوز على ذلك التقدير يكون طلبه للرؤية عبثاً وذلك على الأنبياء محال (۲) مع أنه نبي كريم من أولى العزم من الرسل ، أرأيت المعتزلي أعرف بالله تعالى منه المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام الدعوة إلى العقائد الدينية الحقة والأعمال الصالحة (۳) وحاصل هذا الإستدلال أن سؤال موسى عليه السلام إياها دليل على أنه كان يعتقد أنه كان جائز الرؤية والوجه الثاني أنه تعالى علق الرؤية بشرط منصور الكون وهو إستقرار الجبل ، فدل على أنه جائز الوجود إذ تعليق الفعل بما هو جائز الوجود ، يدل على جوازه ، كما أن التعليق بما هو ممتنع الوجود أو متحقق الوجود يدل على إقناعه ، أو تحققه ، والدليل على أن إستقرار الجبل ممكن الثبوت قوله تعالى : ( فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى رَبُّهُ لِلْجَبَل جَعَلَهُ دَكّاً ) : أخبر أنه جعله دكا ، لا أنه أندل بنفسه ، وما أوجده الله تعالى كان جائزاً أن لا يوجد لو لم يوجده الله تعالى ، إذ الله تعالى مختار فيما يفعل فإذا جعل الجبل دكا باختياره ، وكان جائزاً أن لا يفعل دل على جواز وجوده .
And by His saying in addressing Musa, peace be upon him: 'You will not see Me' (Qur'an 7:143). I wonder how the Mu'tazilite knows from the attributes of the Lord of lords what Musa, peace be upon him, was ignorant of? And how did Musa, peace be upon him, ask for vision while it is impossible? Perhaps ignorance of heretics and whims of ignorant fools is more befitting than ignorance of the prophets, may God's blessings be upon them. (1) From the verse (143) of Surah Al-A'raf (7) and its completion: 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in its place, then you will see Me.' And the aspect of inference is from two perspectives: First: That if vision were not permissible, Musa would not have asked for it, and the consequent is false by consensus and the widespread reports (tawatur). The explanation of the entailment is that Musa, peace be upon him, was knowledgeable of what is permissible for God and what is impossible for Him; otherwise ignorance would be entailed, which is impossible for prophets. And if he was knowledgeable that vision is not permissible, then on that assumption his request for vision would be futile, and that is impossible for prophets. (2) With that he is a noble prophet from the determined messengers (ulu al-azm). Do you think the Mu'tazilite knows more about God than him? The purpose of sending the prophets, may peace and blessings be upon them, is the call to true religious beliefs and righteous deeds. (3) The summary of this inference is that Musa's asking for it is evidence that he believed it was permissible. And the second aspect: that He, exalted, conditioned vision on a condition of possible existence, which is the stability of the mountain. So it indicates that it is possible in existence, because conditioning an action on what is possible indicates its possibility, just as conditioning on what is impossible or actual indicates its impossibility or actuality. And the evidence that the stability of the mountain is possible in establishedness is His saying: 'When his Lord manifested to the mountain, He made it level' (Qur'an 7:143). He informed that He made it level, not that it collapsed by itself. And what God brought into existence was possible not to exist if God had not brought it into existence, for God is free in what He does. So if He made the mountain level by His choice, and it was possible that He not do so, this indicates the possibility of its existence.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite denial of the vision of Allah in the afterlife. Al-Ghazali presents two proofs from the story of Prophet Musa (Moses) in the Qur'an. First proof: Musa asked to see Allah. Since Musa was a knowledgeable prophet aware of what is permissible and impossible for Allah, he would not have asked for something impossible. Therefore, vision must be possible. Second proof: Allah conditioned the vision on the mountain's stability. If the mountain's stability is possible (as proven by the fact that Allah made it level, an act of His free will), then the conditioned thing (vision) is also possible. The passage emphasises that ignorance of heretics is less blameworthy than attributing ignorance to prophets.

Key Terms
رؤية (vision)
إستدلال (inference)
جواز (permissibility)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Musa's request shows he was ignorant or doubted Allah.
Correction: The proof argues the opposite: his request demonstrates his knowledge that vision is possible; a prophet would not ask for the impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is it impossible that vision of Allah is impossible?
A) Because Musa was a prophet and would not ask for something impossible.
B) Because the Qur'an explicitly states it is possible.
C) Because the Mu'tazilites agree it is possible.
D) Because the mountain's stability proves it.

Key Takeaway
A prophet's request for vision proves its possibility, as prophets are infallible and knowledgeable of Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your longing for Allah in this life reflect a deep belief that He is truly accessible, or have you accepted a distant relationship as normal? If a prophet could ask to see Him, what holds you back from asking for more of Him?
?
When you read the verse 'You will not see Me,' does your heart immediately close the door, or do you, like Musa, persist in hope? What would change if you truly believed that seeing Allah is possible?
?
If a prophet's certainty about Allah's attributes led him to ask for the seemingly impossible, what does your own hesitation in asking big things of Allah reveal about your conviction in His power and generosity?

The Second Proof: Conditional Statement Indicates Possibility
Second proof: conditional statement shows possibility of vision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali uses the conditional statement in the verse to infer that vision of Allah is possible, based on the possibility of the mountain's stability.

Core Idea
A condition using a possible thing indicates the possibility of the contingent.

In the second proof, al-Ghazali focuses on the conditional structure of Allah's words to Musa: 'if the mountain remains stable, then you will see Me.' He argues that if the condition (the mountain's stability) is possible, then the conditioned (vision) is also possible. He then demonstrates that the mountain's stability is indeed possible, as proven by the fact that Allah made it level by His own free will, not by collapse.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage about the second proof and its translation. Notice the logical structure.
The key is the conditional 'if...then'.
2
Understand the conditional logic
Al-Ghazali states that conditioning an act on something possible indicates the possibility of the act. Identify the condition and the conditioned.
Condition: stability of the mountain (possible). Conditioned: vision of Allah.
3
Examine the evidence for possibility
The mountain's stability is possible because Allah made it level by choice; it did not collapse by itself. What God brings into existence could have not existed if He willed.
This shows the mountain's stability is contingent and thus possible.

?

?

?

Do you rely on His promise of vision based on logical possibility, or do you have a personal relationship with the One who made that promise?

Vision as Knowledge and Refutation of Conditions
Al-Ghazali defines vision as knowledge and refutes Mu'tazilite conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's definition of vision as a more complete form of knowledge and why it does not require physical conditions like direction or distance.

Core Idea
Vision is perfect knowledge; if Allah can be known without direction, He can be seen without direction.

Al-Ghazali begins by invoking the literal interpretation of Qur'anic verses on seeing Allah. He defines vision as a type of unveiling and knowledge, but more complete than ordinary knowledge. Since knowledge of Allah is possible without spatial conditions, vision—being a higher knowledge—is also possible without them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of vision
Read the opening where al-Ghazali defines vision as a type of unveiling and knowledge, emphasizing that it is more complete and clear than ordinary knowledge.
Notice that he sets up vision as a kind of knowledge, not a separate physical phenomenon.
2
Follow the analogy with knowledge
Al-Ghazali argues that if knowledge of Allah is possible without direction, then vision—being a superior knowledge—must also be possible without direction. This is the core logical move.
3
Identify the three conditions refuted
The passage refutes three conditions: facing (muqabala), special distance, and encompassing (ihata). Locate each in the text and see how al-Ghazali argues against them.
These are the conditions set by the Mu'tazila and philosophers.
4
Examine the proofs
Al-Ghazali provides two proofs: the hadith of the Prophet seeing behind his back (showing vision without facing/distance) and the example of seeing the sky without encompassing it (showing vision without full comprehension).
These proofs are meant to show that vision can occur without physical conditions.
5
Conclude with the overall argument
Reflect on how al-Ghazali establishes the rational possibility of seeing Allah without direction or other physical constraints, based on the nature of vision as knowledge and the divine custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجه إجراء آية الرؤية على الظاهر ، فهو أنه غير مؤد الى المحال (۱) . فإن الرؤية نوع كشف وعلم (۲) ، إلا أنه أتم وأوضح من العلم(۳) ، فإذا جاز تعلق العلم به وليس في جهة جاز تعلق الرؤية به وليس بجهة (٤) (۱) فوجب أن لا يعدل عن الظاهر إذ العدول إنما يجوز عند عدم إمكانه لامع إمكانه ثم علل قوله غير مؤد إلى المحال . (۲) للمدرك بالمرئي يخلق الله هذا النوع عند مقابلة الحاسة للمرئي بحسب ما جرت به العادة الإلهية . (۳) أي أن مسمى الرؤية هو الإدراك المشتمل على الزيادة على الإدراك الذي هو علم جلى ، إذ هو العلم الذي لا ينقص منه قدر من الإدراك (٤) يعني من غير مقابلة بين الباصرة والمرئي في جهة مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة بين الحاسة والمرئي الكائن في تلك الجهة ، ومن غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي وقوله من غير مقابلة : فيه دفع لقول المعتزلة والحكماء القائلين بأن من شرائط الرؤية مقابلة المرئي للباصرة في جهة من جهات ؛ وقولي مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة رد على قولهم ، إن من شرائط الرؤية عدم غاية البعد بحيث ينقطع إدراك الباصرة وعدم غاية القرب ، فإن المبصر إذا التصق بسطح البصر بطل إدراكه بالكلية ، ولذلك لا يرى باطن الأجفان وقولي : من غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي، إشارة نفي كون الرؤية نستلزم الإحاطة بالمرئي لتكون ممتنعة في حقه تعالى ، لأنه لا يحاط به ، قال تعالى : لا يحيطون به علما * والحاصل أنه يجوز عقلاً أن يخلق القدر المذكور من العلم في الحي على وفق مشيئته تعالى ، من غير مقابلة لجهة أخرى وقولي بمجموع المرئي ، فيه تنبيه على أنه إذا ثبت أن المجموع المتركب من أجزاء متناهية ، يرى دون إحاطة ، فالذات المنزهه عن التركيب والتناهي والحدود والجهة أولى بأن تنفك رؤيتها عن الإحاطة ، والدليل على جواز أن يخلق الله قدراً من العلم من غير مقابلة بحاسة البصر أصلاً ، ما ورد في الصحيحين من حديث أنس رفعه : « أتموا صفوفكم فإني أراكم من وراء ظهري وعند البخاري وحده عن أنس : أقيموا صفوفكم وتراصوا » وعند النسائي : ( أستووا ، إستووا فوالذي نفسي بيده إني أراكم من خلفي كما أراكم من بين يدي . والدليل على قولنا من غير إحاطة ، ورءيتنا السماء فانا نراها ولا نحيط بها
As for the aspect of applying the verse of vision (āyat al-ru'ya) according to its apparent meaning, it is that it does not lead to impossibility (1). Indeed, vision is a type of unveiling and knowledge (2), except that it is more complete and clearer than [ordinary] knowledge (3). So if it is permissible for knowledge to be related to Him without [Him being in] a direction, then it is permissible for vision to be related to Him without [Him being in] a direction (4). (1) So it is necessary not to deviate from the apparent meaning, for deviation is only permissible when [the apparent meaning] is impossible, not when it is possible. Then he explained his statement 'it does not lead to impossibility'. (2) [Vision is] perception by the seer; God creates this type when the sense confronts the seen object according to the divine custom. (3) That is, the meaning of 'vision' is perception that includes an increase over the perception which is clear knowledge (ʿilm jalī), for it is knowledge from which no amount of perception is deficient. (4) That is, without any facing between the eye and the seen object in a direction, and without [the condition of] a special distance between the sense and the seen object located in that direction, and without encompassing the entirety of the seen object. His statement 'without facing' contains a refutation of the saying of the Muʿtazila and philosophers who hold that among the conditions of vision is that the seen object face the eye in some direction. And my statement 'with that facing a special distance' is a refutation of their saying that among the conditions of vision is the absence of extreme distance that would cut off the eye's perception and the absence of extreme proximity, for if the seen object is attached to the surface of the eye, its perception is entirely nullified, and therefore the interior of the eyelids is not seen. And my statement: 'without encompassing the entirety of the seen object' is an indication of negating that vision entails encompassing the seen object, so that it would be impossible with respect to Him, exalted, because He cannot be encompassed. He said: 'They cannot encompass Him in knowledge' (Qur'an 20:110). The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible that God create the mentioned amount of knowledge in the living being according to His will, without [the condition of] facing of another direction. And my statement 'of the entirety of the seen object' contains a reminder that if it is established that the composite whole made up of finite parts can be seen without encompassing, then the essence that is free from composition, finitude, limits, and direction is more worthy that its vision be free from encompassing. And the proof for the permissibility that God create an amount of knowledge without any facing of the sense of sight at all is what is reported in the two Sahihs from the hadith of Anas, marfuʿ: 'Complete your rows, for I see you from behind my back.' And in al-Bukhari alone from Anas: 'Straighten your rows and stand shoulder to shoulder.' And in al-Nasa'i: 'Be straight, be straight, for by the One in whose hand my soul is, I see you from behind me as I see you from in front of me.' And the proof for our statement of 'without encompassing' is our seeing the sky, for we see it but we do not encompass it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the literal interpretation of the Qur'anic verses that indicate the possibility of seeing Allah. Al-Ghazali defines vision as a type of unveiling and knowledge, but more complete than ordinary knowledge. Since knowledge of Allah is possible without spatial direction, vision—being a higher form of knowledge—is also possible without direction. He then refutes the conditions posited by the Mu'tazilites and philosophers: (1) facing between the eye and the object, (2) a specific distance, and (3) encompassing the object. He uses two proofs: the hadith of the Prophet seeing behind his back (showing vision without facing), and the example of seeing the sky without encompassing it (showing vision without full comprehension). The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible for Allah to create this type of knowledge in the seer without physical conditions.

Key Terms
رؤية (vision)
علم (knowledge)
إحاطة (encompassing)
مقابلة (facing)
جهة (direction)
عادة إلهية (divine custom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision of Allah requires physical conditions like direction, distance, and facing.
Correction: Al-Ghazali argues that vision is a direct act of Allah creating knowledge in the seer, independent of physical conditions, as shown by the Prophet's vision behind his back and seeing the sky without encompassing it.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason al-Ghazali gives for it being possible to see Allah without direction?
A) Because vision is a type of knowledge and knowledge of Allah is possible without direction.
B) Because the Prophet saw behind his back.
C) Because the sky is seen without being encompassed.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites accepted this.

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah is rationally possible without direction or distance because it is a direct act of divine creation, similar to knowledge but more complete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If vision of Allah is the most complete knowledge, do you truly seek to know Him, or are you satisfied with superficial understanding? What are you doing today to deepen that knowledge?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that Allah can be seen without physical conditions – yet you have never seen Him. Does this truth make you long for that vision, or do you feel distant? Ask yourself: what in your life is keeping you from yearning for that encounter?
?
The Prophet ﷺ saw people behind his back – a sign that vision is not limited by normal physical laws. If you really believed that Allah sees you at all times, without any barrier, would you change one specific habit before the end of this day? Name it and commit to it now.

The Principle of Literal Interpretation and the Nature of Ru'ya
Ghazali introduces the rule for literal interpretation and defines vision as a type of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali defends the literal interpretation of ru'ya based on the principle of non-impossibility and his definition of vision as a more complete form of knowledge.

Core Idea
If knowledge of Allah is possible without direction, vision—a more complete knowledge—must also be possible.

Ghazali begins by establishing a key principle: the literal meaning of scripture should not be abandoned unless it leads to impossibility. He then examines the nature of ru'ya (vision) and argues that it is essentially a type of knowledge, albeit more complete. This allows him to conclude that since knowledge of Allah is possible without direction, vision of Him in the afterlife is also possible without physical conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from principle to definition to conclusion.
The passage includes commentary markers (1), (2), (3) which are explanatory.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the sentence stating that deviation from apparent meaning is only allowed when the literal leads to impossibility. This is the foundation of Ghazali's argument.
The phrase 'العدول إنما يجوز عند عدم إمكانه' captures this.
3
Understand the definition of ru'ya
Ghazali defines ru'ya as a type of knowledge (kashf and 'ilm) that is more complete and clearer. How does this definition support his conclusion?
Compare with ordinary knowledge to see the analogy.
4
Trace the logical inference
Follow the argument: if knowledge of Allah is possible without direction, then vision (a superior knowledge) must also be possible without direction. Write this syllogism in your own words.
This is the core analogy.

وأما وجه إجراء آية الرؤية على الظاهر ، فهو أنه غير مؤد الى المحال (۱) . فإن الرؤية نوع كشف وعلم (۲) ، إلا أنه أتم وأوضح من العلم(۳) ، فإذا جاز تعلق العلم به وليس في جهة جاز تعلق الرؤية به وليس بجهة (٤) (۱) فوجب أن لا يعدل عن الظاهر إذ العدول إنما يجوز عند عدم إمكانه لامع إمكانه ثم علل قوله غير مؤد إلى المحال . (۲) للمدرك بالمرئي يخلق الله هذا النوع عند مقابلة الحاسة للمرئي بحسب ما جرت به العادة الإلهية . (۳) أي أن مسمى الرؤية هو الإدراك المشتمل على الزيادة على الإدراك الذي هو علم جلى ، إذ هو العلم الذي لا ينقص منه قدر من الإدراك (٤) يعني من غير مقابلة بين الباصرة والمرئي في جهة مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة بين الحاسة والمرئي الكائن في تلك الجهة ، ومن غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي وقوله من غير مقابلة : فيه دفع لقول المعتزلة والحكماء القائلين بأن من شرائط الرؤية مقابلة المرئي للباصرة في جهة من جهات ؛ وقولي مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة رد على قولهم ، إن من شرائط الرؤية عدم غاية البعد بحيث ينقطع إدراك الباصرة وعدم غاية القرب ، فإن المبصر إذا التصق بسطح البصر بطل إدراكه بالكلية ، ولذلك لا يرى باطن الأجفان وقولي : من غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي، إشارة نفي كون الرؤية نستلزم الإحاطة بالمرئي لتكون ممتنعة في حقه تعالى ، لأنه لا يحاط به ، قال تعالى : لا يحيطون به علما * والحاصل أنه يجوز عقلاً أن يخلق القدر المذكور من العلم في الحي على وفق مشيئته تعالى ، من غير مقابلة لجهة أخرى وقولي بمجموع المرئي ، فيه تنبيه على أنه إذا ثبت أن المجموع المتركب من أجزاء متناهية ، يرى دون إحاطة ، فالذات المنزهه عن التركيب والتناهي والحدود والجهة أولى بأن تنفك رؤيتها عن الإحاطة ، والدليل على جواز أن يخلق الله قدراً من العلم من غير مقابلة بحاسة البصر أصلاً ، ما ورد في الصحيحين من حديث أنس رفعه : « أتموا صفوفكم فإني أراكم من وراء ظهري وعند البخاري وحده عن أنس : أقيموا صفوفكم وتراصوا » وعند النسائي : ( أستووا ، إستووا فوالذي نفسي بيده إني أراكم من خلفي كما أراكم من بين يدي . والدليل على قولنا من غير إحاطة ، ورءيتنا السماء فانا نراها ولا نحيط بها

وكما يجوز أن يرى الله تعالى الخلق (۱) ، وليس في مقابلتهم ، جاز أن يراه الخلق من غير مقابلة (۲) وكما جاز أن يعلم من غير كيفية وصورة (۳) ، جاز أن يرى كذلك (۱) أي كون ذلك القدر من العلم المسمى بالرؤية مشبهاً في كونه دون مقابلة رؤية الله تعالى إيانا فإنه تعالى يرى خلقه ، (۲) فالرؤية نسبة خاصة بين طرفي راء مرئي ، فإن فرض أن تلك النسبة تقتضي عقلا كون أحدهما من جهة اقتضت كون طرفها الآخر كذلك في جهة لإشتراكهما في التعلق ، فإذا ثبت برفاق الخصمين عدم لزوم ذلك في أحد طرفيها لزم في الطرف الآخر مثله فكان الثابت عقلاً نقيض ما فرض فثبت إنتفاء ما فرض ، وإن فرض اللزوم في أحد الطرفين وعدمه فهو تحكم محض (۳) لأن الرؤية نوع علم خاص يخلقه الله تعالى في الحي غير مشروط بمقابلة ولا غيرها مما ذكر
As for the manner of applying the verse of vision (ru'ya) to its apparent meaning, it is that it does not lead to impossibility (muḥāl). For vision is a type of unveiling (kashf) and knowledge ('ilm), except that it is more complete and clearer than knowledge. So if knowledge can be related to Him without direction (jiha), then vision can be related to Him without direction. (1) Therefore it is necessary not to deviate from the apparent meaning, because deviation is only permissible when it is impossible, not when it is possible. Then he explained his statement 'does not lead to impossibility'. (2) For the perceiver of the visible, God creates this type when the sense faces the visible according to divine custom. (3) That is, the meaning of vision is perception that includes an increase over the perception which is clear knowledge, for it is knowledge from which no amount of perception is lacking. (4) That is, without facing between the seer and the visible in a direction, and without a special distance between the sense and the visible located in that direction, and without encompassing (iḥāṭa) the entirety of the visible. And his statement 'without facing': in it is a refutation of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers who say that among the conditions of vision is the facing of the visible to the seer in some direction. And my statement 'with that facing a special distance' is a reply to their statement that among the conditions of vision is the absence of extreme distance such that the seer's perception is cut off, and the absence of extreme proximity, for if the seen object clings to the surface of the eye, its perception is entirely nullified, and therefore one does not see the inner side of the eyelids. And my statement 'without encompassing the entirety of the visible' is an indication of negating that vision necessitates encompassing the visible, so that it would be impossible regarding Him, because He cannot be encompassed. He said: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' (Qur'an 20:110). The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible for God to create the mentioned amount of knowledge in the living being according to His will, without facing any other direction. And my statement 'of the entirety of the visible' contains a hint that if it is established that a composite whole of finite parts is seen without encompassing, then the essence transcendent above composition, finitude, limits, and direction is more worthy that its vision be free from encompassing. The proof for the permissibility of God creating an amount of knowledge without facing the sense of sight at all is what is reported in the two Sahihs from the hadith of Anas raised: 'Complete your rows, for I see you from behind my back.' And according to al-Bukhari alone from Anas: 'Straighten your rows and be close together.' And according to al-Nasa'i: 'Be straight, be straight, for by Him in whose hand is my soul, I see you from behind me as I see you from in front of me.' And the proof for our statement 'without encompassing' is our seeing the sky, for we see it but do not encompass it. And just as it is permissible for God to see the creation, without facing them, it is permissible for the creation to see Him without facing. And just as He can be known without modality (kayfiyya) and form (ṣūra), He can be seen likewise. (1) That is, that amount of knowledge called vision is similar in being without facing to God's vision of us, for He sees His creation. (2) For vision is a specific relation between two sides, seer and seen. If it is supposed that this relation rationally requires that one of them be in a direction, it would require the other side to be likewise in a direction due to their shared connection. So if, with the agreement of the two opponents, it is established that this necessity does not apply to one of its two sides, then it applies to the other side likewise, and thus what was supposed is rationally negated. And if the necessity is supposed for one side and not the other, it is sheer arbitrariness. (3) Because vision is a specific type of knowledge that God creates in the living being, not conditioned by facing or anything else mentioned.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and commentary defending the literal interpretation of the Quranic verse about seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author begins by stating the principle: interpreting the verse literally does not lead to impossibility (muḥāl). This is the key criterion—deviation from the apparent meaning is only allowed when the literal meaning is impossible, and here it is not. The author then defines ru'ya as a type of unveiling and knowledge, but more complete than ordinary knowledge. Since knowledge of Allah is possible without direction (jiha), vision, being a kind of knowledge, is also possible without direction. The commentary expands on each point: (1) explains why deviation is not needed; (2) describes how ordinary vision occurs through divine custom; (3) clarifies that ru'ya is a perception with an increase over mere knowledge; (4) refutes conditions set by Mu'tazila and philosophers such as facing, distance, and encompassing. The author then provides proofs: from the hadith where the Prophet saw his companions from behind his back (showing vision without facing), and from seeing the sky without encompassing it. He argues by analogy: if Allah sees His creation without facing them, then they can see Him without facing. Finally, he concludes that since Allah can be known without modality and form, He can be seen likewise, because ru'ya is a special type of knowledge created by God unconditioned by physical prerequisites.

Key Terms
رؤية
علم
كشف
محال
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The literal interpretation of ru'ya must be abandoned because it implies direction or physical conditions for Allah.
Correction: Ghazali argues that since vision is a type of knowledge, and knowledge of Allah is possible without direction, vision is also possible without direction. The literal interpretation does not lead to impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does Ghazali say the apparent meaning of the ru'ya verse should not be abandoned?
A) Because the apparent meaning is the most eloquent.
B) Because it does not lead to impossibility, and deviation is only allowed when the literal is impossible.
C) Because the Mu'tazila and philosophers agree with it.
D) Because there is a consensus of the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
The literal interpretation of ru'ya is valid because vision is a type of knowledge that, like knowledge of Allah, does not require direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you encounter a difficult verse about Allah, are you quick to resort to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid discomfort, or do you let the apparent meaning challenge your assumptions? Ask yourself: Am I trusting Allah's words or my own limited reason? Commit to sitting with one such verse before seeking an allegorical escape.
?
Ghazali argues that vision is a superior form of knowledge. In your pursuit of Islamic knowledge, do you prioritize the kind of 'seeing' that transforms the heart, or are you satisfied with accumulating facts? Identify one area where you need to move from knowing about Allah to truly 'seeing' Him in your life, and plan one concrete step toward that.

Refuting Conditions of Vision and Proofs from Hadith and Analogy
Ghazali refutes physical conditions for vision and provides proofs from hadith and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the conditions of facing, distance, and encompassing for seeing Allah, and the proofs he provides from hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is possible without facing, distance, or encompassing, as proven by prophetic hadith and the analogy of seeing the sky.

Ghazali continues by systematically refuting the conditions that the Mu'tazila and philosophers impose on vision: facing (muqābala), appropriate distance, and encompassing (iḥāṭa). He provides evidence from the Prophet's hadith about seeing from behind his back, and from the common experience of seeing the sky without encompassing it. He then uses an analogy: just as Allah sees His creation without facing them, creation can see Him without facing. Finally, he concludes that vision is a special type of knowledge created by God unconditioned by physical prerequisites.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the refuted conditions
Read the passage and note the three conditions Ghazali refutes: facing, distance, and encompassing. Underline the Arabic terms: مقابلة, مسافة, إحاطة.
These are the conditions set by Mu'tazila and philosophers.
2
Analyze the first proof: Hadith of the Prophet
Ghazali cites the hadith where the Prophet says he sees his companions from behind his back. How does this prove vision without facing?
The hadith shows that vision can occur without the seer facing the seen object.
3
Analyze the second proof: Seeing the sky
Ghazali uses the example of seeing the sky without encompassing it. How does this negate the condition of encompassing?
We see the sky as a whole but do not encompass every part.
4
Understand the analogy between God and creation
Ghazali argues that if God can see His creation without facing them, then creation can see God without facing. Work through the logic of this symmetrical relation.
The relation of vision is mutual; if one side does not require facing, the other side also does not.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that vision is a specific type of knowledge created by God, not conditioned by facing or any physical condition. Write down how this answers the objection.
This ties back to the definition of ru'ya as a kind of knowledge.

وأما وجه إجراء آية الرؤية على الظاهر ، فهو أنه غير مؤد الى المحال (۱) . فإن الرؤية نوع كشف وعلم (۲) ، إلا أنه أتم وأوضح من العلم(۳) ، فإذا جاز تعلق العلم به وليس في جهة جاز تعلق الرؤية به وليس بجهة (٤) (۱) فوجب أن لا يعدل عن الظاهر إذ العدول إنما يجوز عند عدم إمكانه لامع إمكانه ثم علل قوله غير مؤد إلى المحال . (۲) للمدرك بالمرئي يخلق الله هذا النوع عند مقابلة الحاسة للمرئي بحسب ما جرت به العادة الإلهية . (۳) أي أن مسمى الرؤية هو الإدراك المشتمل على الزيادة على الإدراك الذي هو علم جلى ، إذ هو العلم الذي لا ينقص منه قدر من الإدراك (٤) يعني من غير مقابلة بين الباصرة والمرئي في جهة مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة بين الحاسة والمرئي الكائن في تلك الجهة ، ومن غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي وقوله من غير مقابلة : فيه دفع لقول المعتزلة والحكماء القائلين بأن من شرائط الرؤية مقابلة المرئي للباصرة في جهة من جهات ؛ وقولي مع تلك المقابلة مسافة خاصة رد على قولهم ، إن من شرائط الرؤية عدم غاية البعد بحيث ينقطع إدراك الباصرة وعدم غاية القرب ، فإن المبصر إذا التصق بسطح البصر بطل إدراكه بالكلية ، ولذلك لا يرى باطن الأجفان وقولي : من غير إحاطة بمجموع المرئي، إشارة نفي كون الرؤية نستلزم الإحاطة بالمرئي لتكون ممتنعة في حقه تعالى ، لأنه لا يحاط به ، قال تعالى : لا يحيطون به علما * والحاصل أنه يجوز عقلاً أن يخلق القدر المذكور من العلم في الحي على وفق مشيئته تعالى ، من غير مقابلة لجهة أخرى وقولي بمجموع المرئي ، فيه تنبيه على أنه إذا ثبت أن المجموع المتركب من أجزاء متناهية ، يرى دون إحاطة ، فالذات المنزهه عن التركيب والتناهي والحدود والجهة أولى بأن تنفك رؤيتها عن الإحاطة ، والدليل على جواز أن يخلق الله قدراً من العلم من غير مقابلة بحاسة البصر أصلاً ، ما ورد في الصحيحين من حديث أنس رفعه : « أتموا صفوفكم فإني أراكم من وراء ظهري وعند البخاري وحده عن أنس : أقيموا صفوفكم وتراصوا » وعند النسائي : ( أستووا ، إستووا فوالذي نفسي بيده إني أراكم من خلفي كما أراكم من بين يدي . والدليل على قولنا من غير إحاطة ، ورءيتنا السماء فانا نراها ولا نحيط بها

وكما يجوز أن يرى الله تعالى الخلق (۱) ، وليس في مقابلتهم ، جاز أن يراه الخلق من غير مقابلة (۲) وكما جاز أن يعلم من غير كيفية وصورة (۳) ، جاز أن يرى كذلك (۱) أي كون ذلك القدر من العلم المسمى بالرؤية مشبهاً في كونه دون مقابلة رؤية الله تعالى إيانا فإنه تعالى يرى خلقه ، (۲) فالرؤية نسبة خاصة بين طرفي راء مرئي ، فإن فرض أن تلك النسبة تقتضي عقلا كون أحدهما من جهة اقتضت كون طرفها الآخر كذلك في جهة لإشتراكهما في التعلق ، فإذا ثبت برفاق الخصمين عدم لزوم ذلك في أحد طرفيها لزم في الطرف الآخر مثله فكان الثابت عقلاً نقيض ما فرض فثبت إنتفاء ما فرض ، وإن فرض اللزوم في أحد الطرفين وعدمه فهو تحكم محض (۳) لأن الرؤية نوع علم خاص يخلقه الله تعالى في الحي غير مشروط بمقابلة ولا غيرها مما ذكر
As for the manner of applying the verse of vision (ru'ya) to its apparent meaning, it is that it does not lead to impossibility (muḥāl). For vision is a type of unveiling (kashf) and knowledge ('ilm), except that it is more complete and clearer than knowledge. So if knowledge can be related to Him without direction (jiha), then vision can be related to Him without direction. (1) Therefore it is necessary not to deviate from the apparent meaning, because deviation is only permissible when it is impossible, not when it is possible. Then he explained his statement 'does not lead to impossibility'. (2) For the perceiver of the visible, God creates this type when the sense faces the visible according to divine custom. (3) That is, the meaning of vision is perception that includes an increase over the perception which is clear knowledge, for it is knowledge from which no amount of perception is lacking. (4) That is, without facing between the seer and the visible in a direction, and without a special distance between the sense and the visible located in that direction, and without encompassing (iḥāṭa) the entirety of the visible. And his statement 'without facing': in it is a refutation of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers who say that among the conditions of vision is the facing of the visible to the seer in some direction. And my statement 'with that facing a special distance' is a reply to their statement that among the conditions of vision is the absence of extreme distance such that the seer's perception is cut off, and the absence of extreme proximity, for if the seen object clings to the surface of the eye, its perception is entirely nullified, and therefore one does not see the inner side of the eyelids. And my statement 'without encompassing the entirety of the visible' is an indication of negating that vision necessitates encompassing the visible, so that it would be impossible regarding Him, because He cannot be encompassed. He said: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' (Qur'an 20:110). The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible for God to create the mentioned amount of knowledge in the living being according to His will, without facing any other direction. And my statement 'of the entirety of the visible' contains a hint that if it is established that a composite whole of finite parts is seen without encompassing, then the essence transcendent above composition, finitude, limits, and direction is more worthy that its vision be free from encompassing. The proof for the permissibility of God creating an amount of knowledge without facing the sense of sight at all is what is reported in the two Sahihs from the hadith of Anas raised: 'Complete your rows, for I see you from behind my back.' And according to al-Bukhari alone from Anas: 'Straighten your rows and be close together.' And according to al-Nasa'i: 'Be straight, be straight, for by Him in whose hand is my soul, I see you from behind me as I see you from in front of me.' And the proof for our statement 'without encompassing' is our seeing the sky, for we see it but do not encompass it. And just as it is permissible for God to see the creation, without facing them, it is permissible for the creation to see Him without facing. And just as He can be known without modality (kayfiyya) and form (ṣūra), He can be seen likewise. (1) That is, that amount of knowledge called vision is similar in being without facing to God's vision of us, for He sees His creation. (2) For vision is a specific relation between two sides, seer and seen. If it is supposed that this relation rationally requires that one of them be in a direction, it would require the other side to be likewise in a direction due to their shared connection. So if, with the agreement of the two opponents, it is established that this necessity does not apply to one of its two sides, then it applies to the other side likewise, and thus what was supposed is rationally negated. And if the necessity is supposed for one side and not the other, it is sheer arbitrariness. (3) Because vision is a specific type of knowledge that God creates in the living being, not conditioned by facing or anything else mentioned.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and commentary defending the literal interpretation of the Quranic verse about seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author begins by stating the principle: interpreting the verse literally does not lead to impossibility (muḥāl). This is the key criterion—deviation from the apparent meaning is only allowed when the literal meaning is impossible, and here it is not. The author then defines ru'ya as a type of unveiling and knowledge, but more complete than ordinary knowledge. Since knowledge of Allah is possible without direction (jiha), vision, being a kind of knowledge, is also possible without direction. The commentary expands on each point: (1) explains why deviation is not needed; (2) describes how ordinary vision occurs through divine custom; (3) clarifies that ru'ya is a perception with an increase over mere knowledge; (4) refutes conditions set by Mu'tazila and philosophers such as facing, distance, and encompassing. The author then provides proofs: from the hadith where the Prophet saw his companions from behind his back (showing vision without facing), and from seeing the sky without encompassing it. He argues by analogy: if Allah sees His creation without facing them, then they can see Him without facing. Finally, he concludes that since Allah can be known without modality and form, He can be seen likewise, because ru'ya is a special type of knowledge created by God unconditioned by physical prerequisites.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah requires physical conditions such as facing, proper distance, and encompassing, just like seeing physical objects.
Correction: Ghazali provides multiple proofs that vision can occur without these conditions: the Prophet saw from behind, we see the sky without encompassing, and Allah sees creation without facing.

Checkpoint
Which of the following did Ghazali NOT refute as a condition for vision?
A) Facing (muqābala)
B) Proper distance (masāfa)
C) Encompassing (iḥāṭa)
D) The existence of a visible object

Key Takeaway
Vision of Allah is possible without facing, distance, or encompassing, as proven by prophetic hadith, experience, and rational analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet could see his companions from behind his back—a miracle that defies physical laws. If Allah can grant vision without any condition, why do you still limit your perception of Him to what your senses can grasp? How will you cultivate a deeper, unseen awareness of His presence starting this moment?
?
You see the sky every day without encompassing it, yet you accept that as vision. But when it comes to seeing Allah, you may instinctively demand conditions that suit your limited experience. Are you willing to let go of that demand and trust that His vision is unlike any other? What is holding you back from truly longing for that encounter?
?
Ghazali concludes that vision is a special type of knowledge created by Allah, unconditioned by physical prerequisites. If your 'seeing' of Allah in this life—through faith and certainty—is also a type of knowledge He creates, are you actively seeking that gift, or are you passively waiting? What concrete step can you take today to increase your certainty of the unseen?

Divine Attributes

The Principle of Oneness: Negating Multiplicity in Essence, Attributes, and Actions
Ghazali defines tawhid as negation of multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that tawhid according to Ghazali involves negating all forms of multiplicity—both connected and discrete—in Allah's essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Tawhid is not merely believing in one God; it is negating every form of multiplicity in His essence, attributes, and actions.

This lesson unpacks the tenth principle of Ghazali's creed: al-wahdaniyya. While many understand tawhid as simply denying other gods, Ghazali shows that it is far more comprehensive. It requires negating any multiplicity—whether composition, duplication of attributes, or sharing in creative acts—in three domains. The passage first defines key terms like 'sharik' and 'nidd,' then systematically explains oneness in essence, attributes, and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three domains
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note that Ghazali first gives a general statement of oneness, then explains it through three domains: essence, attributes, and actions.
Pay attention to the terms 'sharik' and 'nidd' in the opening.
2
Distinguish connected vs. discrete multiplicity
For each domain, Ghazali negates two types of multiplicity: connected (internal composition) and discrete (external resemblance). Find these distinctions in the passage.
Connected multiplicity in essence means being composed of parts; discrete means having a separate essence similar to His.
3
Reflect on the exclusivity of creative acts
Notice how oneness of actions negates that any act truly belongs to another. The final commentary clarifies that 'ikhtira' (invention), 'ijad' (bringing into existence), and 'khalq' (creation) are exclusive to Allah.
This shows that tawhid extends to denying any true creative power to creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل العاشر : الوحدانية . العلم بأن الله عز وجل واحد لا شريك له (٤) ، فرد لا ند(٥) له ، الفرد بالخلق والإبداع واستبد بالإيجاد والإختراع (٦) ، لا مثل له يساهمه ويساويه، ولا

(٤) الشريك فعيل من الشركة وهو كون الشيء بحيث يتحد مع غيره في شيء موضوعاً كان أو محمولاً صفة أو موصوفاً متعلقاً أو أثراً .
(٥) لا تشبيه له ، ثم إن الوحدانية هي الصفة الخامسة من الصفات السلبية ، وهي عبارة عن سلب التعدد في الذات والصفات والأفعال ، فوحدانية الذات تنفي التعدد المتصل بأن يكون ذاتاً مركبة من جواهر وأعراض، والتعدد المنفصل بأن تكون ذات تماثل ذاته ووحدانية الصفات تنفي التعدد المتصل بأن تكون له قدرتان وارداتان وارادتان وعلمان فأكثر إلى آخرها والتعدد المنفصل بأن تكون صفة في ذات تماثل صفاته الأزلية ، ووحدانية الأفعال تنفي أن يكون فعل أو إختراع أو إيجاد لغيره تعالى من الممكنات حقيقة .
(٦) والإختراع خاص بالله عز وجل والفعل يطلق على القديم والحادث إلا أنه في حق الله تعالى لأنه هو الذي اخترعه ، وأما في حق الحادث فمجاز وإنما عبارة عن مباشرتهم للأشياء وتحريكهم لها والإيجاد والخلق أيضاً خاصان بالله تعالى
The tenth principle: Oneness (al-waḥdāniyya). Knowledge that Allah Almighty is one, having no partner (sharīk), unique (fard), having no equal (nidd), unique in creation and origination (ibdā'), exclusive in bringing into existence (ījād) and invention (ikhtirā'). He has no like to share with Him or equal Him. (4) Al-sharīk is from partnership, meaning a thing such that it unites with another in something, whether subject or predicate, attribute or described, relation or effect. (5) He has no likeness. Then, al-waḥdāniyya is the fifth of the negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya), and it is the negation of multiplicity (ta'addud) in essence, attributes, and actions. The oneness of essence negates connected multiplicity such that the essence would be composed of substances and accidents, and discrete multiplicity such that there would be an essence resembling His essence. The oneness of attributes negates connected multiplicity such that He would have two powers, two wills, two knowledges, or more, etc., and discrete multiplicity such that there would be an attribute in an essence resembling His eternal attributes. The oneness of actions negates that any act, invention, or bringing into existence truly be for other than Him from among contingent things. (6) Al-ikhtirā' is specific to Allah Almighty. The term 'act' (fi'l) is applied to the eternal and the temporal, but regarding Allah it is because He invented it; as for the temporal, it is metaphorical and only denotes their direct engagement with things and their movement of them. And bringing into existence (ījād) and creation (khalq) are also specific to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage defines the tenth principle of al-Ghazali's creed: al-waḥdāniyya (oneness). It begins by stating that we must know that Allah is one, having no partner (sharīk), unique (fard), and without equal (nidd). The commentary then explains each term: 'sharīk' means sharing in any aspect—subject, predicate, attribute, relation, or effect. Then the author explains that 'nidd' means no likeness. The central definition follows: al-waḥdāniyya is the fifth of the negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya), i.e., attributes that negate imperfections. It consists of negating multiplicity (ta'addud) in three domains: essence, attributes, and actions. Oneness of essence negates two types of multiplicity: 1) connected multiplicity, i.e., composition from substances and accidents; 2) discrete multiplicity, i.e., a separate essence similar to His. Oneness of attributes also negates two types: 1) connected multiplicity, e.g., having two powers, two wills, two knowledges, etc.; 2) discrete multiplicity, i.e., an attribute in some other essence resembling His eternal attributes. Oneness of actions negates that any act, invention (ikhtirā'), or bringing into existence (ījād) truly belongs to other than Allah among contingent things. The final commentary clarifies that 'ikhtirā' (invention), 'ījād' (bringing into existence), and 'khalq' (creation) are specific to Allah. The term 'act' (fi'l) is applied to Allah in a real sense because He invents it, while for created beings it is metaphorical, referring only to their direct manipulation of already existing things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tawhid only means denying multiple gods, ignoring negation of composition, duplication of attributes, and creatorship.
Correction: Ghazali shows that tawhid (waḥdāniyya) also requires negating compositional multiplicity in essence, multiple identical attributes, and attributes resembling His, as well as any true creative act by other than Allah.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'oneness of attributes' (waḥdāniyyat al-ṣifāt) negate?
A) That Allah has any attributes.
B) That there could be multiple attributes of the same kind (e.g., two powers) in Allah, or an attribute in another resembling His.
C) That attributes are distinct from the essence.
D) That attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Oneness of Allah means He is free from any multiplicity—whether internal composition or external resemblance—in His essence, attributes, and actions, and that true creative power belongs to Him alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If tawhid means that no act of invention or creation truly belongs to any other than Allah, then how often do you catch yourself thinking that your own efforts 'create' results? In what area of your life do you need to repent of attributing creative power to yourself or to causes other than Allah, and how will you shift that belief today?
?
When you reflect on the oneness of attributes—that Allah has no multiple identical attributes and no attribute like His—does your heart acknowledge that His knowledge, power, and will are utterly unique? Have you ever fallen into the trap of imagining Allah's attributes as similar to human attributes, even slightly? What specific thought or doubt about Allah's attributes have you rationalized, and how will you replace it with the affirmation of His absolute uniqueness?
?
The oneness of essence negates composition and resemblance. Is your understanding of Allah truly free from any mental image or composition? Examine your heart: do you unwittingly picture Allah as having parts or as being like something you know? What step will you take today to purify your tawhid from such imaginings?

Divine Attributes

The First Principle: God's Attribute of Power
Ghazali defines God's attribute of power and its derived names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's attribute of power (qudra), the evidence for it from the order of the world, the meaning of 'al-Qadir' and 'al-Qadir al-Mutlaq', and the nuances of the divine names derived from power.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited by His will—He can do what He does not will.

Imam Ghazali begins the first principle of Islamic creed by establishing the attribute of power. He shows that the very order of the world proves its Maker is powerful, and then clarifies that power is not the same as actual doing. This passage also distinguishes between different divine names derived from power, teaching us about the relationship between God's attributes and His names.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence from the world's order
Read the opening lines where Ghazali argues that the world's solid workmanship and arrangement prove that its Maker is powerful. Notice the analogy of a well-woven silk garment.
2
Learn the definition of al-Qadir
Read the definition: al-Qadir is one who does if He wills and does not do if He does not will. Understand that power is conditional on will, not on actual action.
The example of the Hour shows God can establish it but does not will it.
3
Understand al-Qadir al-Mutlaq
Read about the absolutely Powerful: the One who creates everything independently and uniquely, without any helper.
4
Examine the divine names
Read the discussion of al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, and al-Qadir. Note the intensity order and the secondary meaning of al-Qadir as 'measured/determined'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الأول : القدرة
العلم بأن صانع العالم قادر (۱) ، وأنه تعالى في قوله :
وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ (۲) صادق ، لأن العالم محكم في
صنعته مرتب في خلقته ومن رأى ثوباً من ديباج حسن النسج والتأليف متناسب
(1) أي ذو قدرة ، وهي عبارة عن المعنى الذي به يوجد الشيء مقدراً بتقدير الإدارة والعلم واقعاً
على وفقهما ؛
فالقادر هو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل ، وليس من شرطة أن يشاء لا محالة ، فإن الله تعالى قادر على إقامة القيامة الآن ، فإنه لو شاء أقامها ، وإن كان لا يقيمها ، فإنه لم يشاءها ولا يشاؤها ، لما جرى في سابق علمه من تقدير أجلها ووقتها ، وذلك لا يقدح في القدرة والقادر المطلق هو الذي يخترع كل موجود اختراعاً ينفرد به ويستغني فيه عن معاونة غيره هو الله
سبحانه وتعالى .
(۲) الآية رقم (۱) من سورة الملك ( ٦٧) .
وقد ورد في السنة الشريفة المطهرة : ذكر القادر والمقتدر في أسماء الله تعالى ، وجاء القرآن بهذين الإسمين بالقدير أيضاً ، والقدير أبلغ من القادر والمقتدر أبلغ من القادر ، وللقادر معنيان يكون بمعنى القدير من القدرة على كل شيء ، وذلك صفة الله عز وجل وحده من دون غيره ، وإنما يوصف القادر منا بالقدرة على بعض المقدورات دون بعض
الوجه الثاني : أن يكون بمعنى المقدور ، يقال قدر بالتخفيف وقدر بالتشديد
The first principle: Power.
Knowledge that the Maker of the world is Powerful (1), and that He, exalted, in His saying: 'And He is over all things Powerful' (2) is truthful, because the world is solidly made in its workmanship, arranged in its creation. And whoever sees a garment of silk of good weave and composition, proportionate... (1) i.e., Possessor of power, which is an expression for the attribute by which a thing exists determined by the determination of will and knowledge, occurring in accordance with them. So the Powerful (al-Qadir) is the One who, if He wills, He does, and if He does not will, He does not do. And it is not a condition that He will necessarily. For indeed Allah, exalted, is powerful over establishing the Hour now, for if He willed He would establish it, although He does not establish it, because He has not willed it and will not will it, due to what has passed in His prior knowledge of the determination of its term and time. And that does not impair power. And the absolutely Powerful (al-Qadir al-Mutlaq) is the One who invents every existent with a unique invention, independent in it and self-sufficient without the assistance of another; He is Allah, glorified and exalted. (2) The verse number (1) from Surah al-Mulk (67). It has been transmitted in the noble purified Sunnah: mentioning al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir among the names of Allah, and the Qur'an has mentioned also by these two names: al-Qadir, and al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir, and al-Muqtadir is more intensive than al-Qadir. And al-Qadir has two meanings: one meaning is synonymous with al-Qadir in the sense of power over everything, and that is an attribute of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, alone without others. For indeed, the powerful one among us is described with power over some possibles but not others. The second aspect: that it may be in the meaning of al-maqdir (the measured/determined); one says 'qadara' (with lightening) and 'qaddara' (with doubling).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defines the first principle of faith concerning God's attribute of power (qudra). The author begins by stating that we must know that the Maker of the world is powerful (qadir), and that He is truthful in His statement 'He is over all things Powerful' (Qur'an 67:1). The evidence is the solid and ordered craftsmanship of the world: just as a well-woven silk garment indicates a skilled weaver, the world's order indicates a powerful Maker.

The footnote explains that 'power' is the attribute by which a thing exists in accordance with the precise determination of will and knowledge. Then the author defines the powerful one (al-qadir) as one who does if He wills and does not do if He does not will. It is not a condition that He always must will—for example, Allah is powerful to establish the Hour now, but He does not will it, so He does not establish it. This does not impair His power.

Then the truly and absolutely Powerful (al-qadir al-mutlaq) is defined as the One who creates every existing thing uniquely, independently, without any helper. This applies solely to Allah.

Next, the author discusses the divine names al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, and al-Qadir. Al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir, and al-Muqtadir is more intensive than al-Qadir. The word 'al-Qadir' can have two meanings: first, it is synonymous with al-Qadir (possessor of power over everything), and this applies only to Allah; second, it may mean 'al-maqdir' (the measured/determined), as in the verb 'qadara' (to measure) and 'qaddara' (to determine with precision). The passage thus clarifies the attribute of power, its evidence, and the nuances of the divine names derived from it.

Key Terms
القدرة
القادر
القدير
المقتدر
القادر المطلق
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God does not will a thing, He is unable to do it.
Correction: Ghazali explains that power is independent of actual will; God can do what He does not will, as in the example of the Hour.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ghazali, what is the definition of al-Qadir (the Powerful)?
A) The One who does everything that is possible
B) The One who does if He wills and does not do if He does not will
C) The One who is capable of doing only what He actually does
D) The One who creates everything without will

Key Takeaway
God's power is absolute and independent of His actual will, and the names al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, and al-Qadir convey different intensities of this attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah could have prevented every hardship you face but chose not to for a wise purpose, how would your response to trials change starting tomorrow?
?
You know that Allah is al-Qadir al-Mutlaq—independent of any helper. But in your daily life, where do you still act as if you need to rely on created means as if they were the real source of power, rather than the One who alone invents and sustains all things?
?
When you recite 'Wa huwa 'ala kulli shay'in Qadir', does it only pass over your tongue, or does it settle in your heart that He can indeed change your situation in an instant—and if it does, what is one decision you would make differently today?

The Proof of Divine Power from Creation
Al-Ghazali proves God's power through the order of creation using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ghazali uses the order of creation as a rational proof for God's power.

Core Idea
A perfect universe cannot come from a powerless source.

Al-Ghazali begins the first principle of faith by establishing that the Maker of the world is powerful. He offers a vivid analogy: just as a finely woven garment cannot be produced by a dead or powerless person, the intricate order of the universe cannot arise without a powerful Creator. This lesson unpacks that proof and its rational force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage describing the garment analogy and understand its literal meaning.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice that Al-Ghazali presents a conditional: if someone believes a garment comes from a powerless source, they are irrational. Extend that same logic to the universe.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why is this proof effective? What does it tell us about the necessity of a powerful Creator?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الأول : القدرة
العلم بأن صانع العالم قادر (۱) ، وأنه تعالى في قوله : وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ (۲) صادق ، لأن العالم محكم في صنعته مرتب في خلقته ومن رأى ثوباً من ديباج حسن النسج والتأليف متناسب التطريز والتطريف ، ثم توهم صدور نسجه عن ميت لا استطاعة له ، او عن انسان لا قدرة له كان منخلعاً عن غريزة العقل ، ومنخرطاً في سلك أهل الغباوة والجهل
(1) أي ذو قدرة ، وهي عبارة عن المعنى الذي به يوجد الشيء مقدراً بتقدير الإدارة والعلم واقعاً على وفقهما ؛ فالقادر هو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل ، وليس من شرطة أن يشاء لا محالة ، فإن الله تعالى قادر على إقامة القيامة الآن ، فإنه لو شاء أقامها ، وإن كان لا يقيمها ، فإنه لم يشاءها ولا يشاؤها ، لما جرى في سابق علمه من تقدير أجلها ووقتها ، وذلك لا يقدح في القدرة والقادر المطلق هو الذي يخترع كل موجود اختراعاً ينفرد به ويستغني فيه عن معاونة غيره هو الله سبحانه وتعالى .
(۲) الآية رقم (۱) من سورة الملك ( ٦٧) .
وقد ورد في السنة الشريفة المطهرة : ذكر القادر والمقتدر في أسماء الله تعالى ، وجاء القرآن بهذين الإسمين بالقدير أيضاً ، والقدير أبلغ من القادر والمقتدر أبلغ من القادر ، وللقادر معنيان يكون بمعنى القدير من القدرة على كل شيء ، وذلك صفة الله عز وجل وحده من دون غيره ، وإنما يوصف القادر منا بالقدرة على بعض المقدورات دون بعض
الوجه الثاني : أن يكون بمعنى المقدور ، يقال قدر بالتخفيف وقدر بالتشديد
The First Principle: Power. The knowledge that the Maker of the world is powerful (1), and that He, exalted, is truthful in His saying: 'And He is over all things Omnipotent' (2), because the world is firmly made in its craftsmanship and ordered in its creation, and whoever sees a garment of brocade with beautiful weaving and composition, proportionate in embroidery and edging, then imagines its weaving to have come from a dead person who has no capacity, or from a human who has no power, would be stripped of the instinct of reason and strung along with the people of dullness and ignorance. (1) i.e., possessing power, which is the attribute by which a thing exists measured by the measurement of will and knowledge actually in accordance with them; so the Powerful One is He who, if He wills, He does, and if He does not will, He does not do, and it is not a condition that He will necessarily, for God, exalted, is able to establish the Hour now, for if He willed, He would establish it, even though He does not establish it, because He did not will it and does not will it, due to what has passed in His prior knowledge of the determination of its term and time, and that does not detract from power. And the absolutely Powerful One is He who invents every existing thing entirely by Himself, unique in it, and independent of the help of others; He is God, glorified and exalted. (2) The verse number (1) from Surah Al-Mulk (67). And it has come in the noble purified Sunnah: mentioning al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir among the names of God, and the Qur'an came with these two names and also with al-Qadir. And al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir is more intensive than al-Qadir. And al-Qadir has two meanings: one is in the sense of al-Qadir of power over everything, and that is the attribute of God, mighty and majestic, alone to the exclusion of others. And one who is powerful among us is described as having power over some of the possible things and not others. The second meaning: that it is in the sense of al-maqdur (the thing over which power is exercised); one says qadura with lightening and qadura with emphasis.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of divine power (qudra). Al-Ghazali begins by stating that we must know that the Maker of the world is powerful (qadir) and truthful in His Quranic statement that He is over all things omnipotent (qadir). The proof is by way of analogy: the world is perfectly ordered and crafted, like a finely woven garment. If someone imagined such a garment came from a dead person or a powerless human, that person would be irrational. So how could the far more perfect universe come from an incapable source? The creator must be powerful. Al-Ghazali then explains the meaning of 'power' (qudra): it is the attribute by which a thing exists measured by will and knowledge. The powerful one is He who acts if He wills and does not act if He does not will; it is not required that He actually will at all times. For example, God is able to establish the Hour now, but He will not because in His prior knowledge He decreed a specific time. This does not diminish His power. The absolutely powerful One is the one who invents every existing thing independently and without assistance; that is God. Then Al-Ghazali discusses the names al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir as found in the Quran and Sunnah, noting that al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? (there is a copying issue: actually al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? Probably al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? He says 'al-Qadir ablaghu min al-Qadir wa al-Muqtadir ablaghu min al-Qadir' which suggests al-Qadir is stronger than al-Qadir? This seems like a text corruption or scribal error; but we follow the text. He says al-Qadir has two meanings: one is the intensive sense of power over everything, which is specific to God; the other is the passive sense of 'that which is measured' (maqdur). This clarifies the nuance of the term.

Key Terms
قدرة
قادر
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the order of the universe could arise from a powerless source or by chance.
Correction: Al-Ghazali argues that just as a beautifully woven garment requires a skilled weaver, the universe requires a powerful Creator. To think otherwise is unreasonable.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali's analogy, what would be the irrational belief about the perfectly woven garment?
A) That it was woven by a skilled weaver
B) That it came from a dead person or a powerless human
C) That it was made by machine
D) That it is beautiful

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali proves God's power by arguing that a perfectly ordered universe cannot arise from a powerless source; such a belief is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order in your own life—your daily routines, your body, your circumstances—do you truly attribute all of that to a powerful Creator, or do you sometimes act as if it came about by chance? What would change if you fully believed in God's power over every detail?
?
Al-Ghazali says denying God's power from creation is like thinking a perfect brocade came from a dead person. In what areas of your life do you subtly deny God's power by worrying or relying on your own strength instead of His? Will you repent and acknowledge His power today?
?
The analogy shows that it is irrational to attribute order to a powerless source. Are there areas of your faith where you hold irrational beliefs about God's power? What step will you take to align your heart with the Quranic truth that He is over all things Omnipotent?

The Meaning of Divine Power and Its Relation to Will
Al-Ghazali defines divine power and explains its relationship to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine power (qudra) and why God's power does not compel Him to act against His will.

Core Idea
God is able to do something even if He does not will it.

After establishing that God is powerful, Al-Ghazali defines what power means and clarifies a common misunderstanding. Power is the attribute by which something exists in accordance with will and knowledge. The powerful one acts if He wills and refrains if He does not. Using the example of the Day of Judgment, Al-Ghazali shows that God is able to bring it now, but He does not will it, so it does not happen. This lesson explores this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qudra
Read the passage where Al-Ghazali defines power as 'the attribute by which a thing exists measured by will and knowledge'.
2
Understand the condition of will
Notice the statement: 'the Powerful One is He who, if He wills, He does, and if He does not will, He does not do'.
This means power is contingent on will.
3
Examine the example of the Hour
Al-Ghazali gives the example of God being able to establish the Hour now but not willing it. Understand why that does not diminish power.

Source Text

The Names of Power and Their Semantic Nuances
Al-Ghazali explains the divine names al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, and their meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir and why they are exclusive to God.

Core Idea
Al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir are intensive forms denoting full power, which belongs only to God.

Al-Ghazali discusses the names of God related to power as found in the Quran and Sunnah. He explains that al-Qadir is more intensive than the basic form, and al-Muqtadir is even more intensive. He also notes that the word al-Qadir has two meanings: one denoting power over everything (exclusive to God) and another denoting the object of power (maqdur). This lesson clarifies these linguistic and theological points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the names mentioned
Read the passage and list the names al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, and al-Qadir.
2
Understand the intensity comparison
Al-Ghazali says al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? The text may have a copying issue. Focus on the statement: al-Qadir is more intensive than the basic form, and al-Muqtadir is more intensive than al-Qadir.
3
Learn the two meanings of al-Qadir
Al-Ghazali distinguishes the active meaning (power over everything) which is God's alone, and the passive meaning (that which is measured/predestined).
