The First Principle: Power. The knowledge that the Maker of the world is powerful (1), and that He, exalted, is truthful in His saying: 'And He is over all things Omnipotent' (2), because the world is firmly made in its craftsmanship and ordered in its creation, and whoever sees a garment of brocade with beautiful weaving and composition, proportionate in embroidery and edging, then imagines its weaving to have come from a dead person who has no capacity, or from a human who has no power, would be stripped of the instinct of reason and strung along with the people of dullness and ignorance. (1) i.e., possessing power, which is the attribute by which a thing exists measured by the measurement of will and knowledge actually in accordance with them; so the Powerful One is He who, if He wills, He does, and if He does not will, He does not do, and it is not a condition that He will necessarily, for God, exalted, is able to establish the Hour now, for if He willed, He would establish it, even though He does not establish it, because He did not will it and does not will it, due to what has passed in His prior knowledge of the determination of its term and time, and that does not detract from power. And the absolutely Powerful One is He who invents every existing thing entirely by Himself, unique in it, and independent of the help of others; He is God, glorified and exalted. (2) The verse number (1) from Surah Al-Mulk (67). And it has come in the noble purified Sunnah: mentioning al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir among the names of God, and the Qur'an came with these two names and also with al-Qadir. And al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir is more intensive than al-Qadir. And al-Qadir has two meanings: one is in the sense of al-Qadir of power over everything, and that is the attribute of God, mighty and majestic, alone to the exclusion of others. And one who is powerful among us is described as having power over some of the possible things and not others. The second meaning: that it is in the sense of al-maqdur (the thing over which power is exercised); one says qadura with lightening and qadura with emphasis.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of divine power (qudra). Al-Ghazali begins by stating that we must know that the Maker of the world is powerful (qadir) and truthful in His Quranic statement that He is over all things omnipotent (qadir). The proof is by way of analogy: the world is perfectly ordered and crafted, like a finely woven garment. If someone imagined such a garment came from a dead person or a powerless human, that person would be irrational. So how could the far more perfect universe come from an incapable source? The creator must be powerful. Al-Ghazali then explains the meaning of 'power' (qudra): it is the attribute by which a thing exists measured by will and knowledge. The powerful one is He who acts if He wills and does not act if He does not will; it is not required that He actually will at all times. For example, God is able to establish the Hour now, but He will not because in His prior knowledge He decreed a specific time. This does not diminish His power. The absolutely powerful One is the one who invents every existing thing independently and without assistance; that is God. Then Al-Ghazali discusses the names al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir as found in the Quran and Sunnah, noting that al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? (there is a copying issue: actually al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? Probably al-Qadir is more intensive than al-Qadir? He says 'al-Qadir ablaghu min al-Qadir wa al-Muqtadir ablaghu min al-Qadir' which suggests al-Qadir is stronger than al-Qadir? This seems like a text corruption or scribal error; but we follow the text. He says al-Qadir has two meanings: one is the intensive sense of power over everything, which is specific to God; the other is the passive sense of 'that which is measured' (maqdur). This clarifies the nuance of the term.

مقدور

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the names al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir are synonyms with no difference.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows they have different levels of intensity and also that al-Qadir has two meanings, one of which is passive.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali, what is the first meaning of al-Qadir?
A) One who has power over some things
B) One who has power over everything
C) The one who is measured
D) The one who wills

Key Takeaway
The names al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir denote intensive power exclusive to God, and al-Qadir also has the passive meaning of that which is measured.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power (al-Qadir) encompasses every detail of your life, including those areas you try to control yourself? What is one thing today that you need to surrender to His absolute power?
?
When unexpected events disrupt your plans, do you remember that every outcome is measured (maqdur) by al-Muqtadir? How can you cultivate a heart of trust in God's precise measurement?
?
The passive meaning of al-Qadir reminds us that God measures everything with perfect wisdom. In what area of your life do you struggle with accepting God's measure for you, and what step can you take this week to submit to it?

The Second Principle: Divine Knowledge and Its Proof
Al-Ghazali defines God's all-encompassing knowledge and proves it from creation, then explains the names al-Latif and al-Khabir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge encompasses all things and is proven by the act of creation, and you will grasp the meanings of the divine names al-Latif and al-Khabir.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not derived from creation; creation is derived from His knowledge.

This lesson establishes the second principle of Islamic theology according to Al-Ghazali: the knowledge of God. The passage presents a Quranic proof from creation and unpacks two of God's beautiful names—al-Latif and al-Khabir—that reveal the depth of His knowledge. Before reading, recall that the first principle covered God's existence. Now we affirm that this God knows all things intimately.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and proof
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Ghazali states that God is knower of all things and cites the verse 'He is of all things Knowing' and then the proof verse from Surah Al-Mulk.
Notice the logic: the Creator must know His creation.
2
Understand the explanation of al-Latif
Read the explanation of the name 'al-Latif' and how it combines subtle knowledge with gentle action.
Al-Latif refers to His knowledge of subtleties and His gentle exercise of power.
3
Understand the explanation of al-Khabir
Read the explanation of 'al-Khabir' as the One who knows inner reports, with no detail hidden from Him.
The word 'khabir' comes from 'khibra' meaning expertise in hidden matters.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari principle
Consider Al-Ghazali's point that God's knowledge is not derived from things but rather things are derived from His knowledge.
This distinguishes God's knowledge from human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني : العلم .
العلم بأنه تعالى عالم بجميع الموجودات ، ومحيط بكل المخلوقات يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة في الأرض ولا في السماء ، صادق في قوله وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ (۱) ومرشد إلى صدقه بقوله تعالى : أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ ﴾ (٢) أرشدك إلى الاستدلال بالخلق على العلم بأنك لا تستريب في دلالة الخلق
(۱) الآية (۱۰۱) من سورة الأنعام (٦) والمعنى: أنه سبحانه ظاهره وباطنه دقيق وجليله أوله وآخره ، عاقبته وخاتمته ، وهذا من حيث الكشف على أتم ما يمكن فيه بحيث لا يتصور مشاهدة وكشف أظهر منه ، ولا يكون مستفاداً من المعلومات بل تكون المعلومات مستفادة منه .
(٢) الآية (١٤) من سورة الملك (٦٧) . والمراد : من يعلم دقائق المصالح وغوامضها وما دق منها وما لطف ثم يسلك في إيصالها إلى المستصلح على سبيل الرفق دون العنق ، فإذا إجتمع الرفق في الفعل واللطف والإدراك ، ثم اللطف ولا يتصور كمال ذلك في العلم والفعل إلا الله تعالى ؛ فأما إحاطته بالدقائق معنی والخفايا فلا يمكن تفصيل ذلك ، بل الخفي عنده كالجلي من غير فرق ، وأما رفقه في الأفعال ولطفه فيها فلا يدخل أيضاً تحت الحصر إذ لا يعرف اللطف في فعله إلا من عرف تفاصيل أفعاله وعرف دقائق اللطف فيها وبقدر إتساع المعرفة فيها تتسع بمعنى إسم اللطيف ؛ وأما الخبير فهو الذي لا تعزب عنه الأخبار الباطنة فلا يجري في الملك والملكوت شيء لا تتحرك ذرة ولا تسكن ولا تضطرب نفس ولا تطمئن إلا ويكون عنده خبرها وهو بمعنى العليم ، إلا أن العلم إذا أضيف الى الخفا ، الباطنة سمي خبرة ويسمى صاحبها خبيراً .
The Second Principle: Knowledge. The knowledge that He, exalted, is knower of all existents, and encompassing of all creatures; not the weight of a particle in the earth or in the heaven escapes His knowledge, truthful in His saying: 'And He is of all things Knowing' (1), and guiding to His truthfulness by His saying, exalted: 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Aware?' (2) He guided you to the inference through creation to knowledge, because you have no doubt in the indication of creation. (1) The verse (101) from Surah Al-An'am (6). And the meaning: that He, glorified, apparent and hidden, subtle and great, beginning and end, consequence and conclusion, and this is from the aspect of uncovering in the most perfect possible way such that a more apparent unveiling cannot be imagined, and it is not derived from the known things, rather the known things are derived from it. (2) The verse (14) from Surah Al-Mulk (67). And the intent: He who knows the subtleties of benefits and their obscurities, and what is subtle and what is fine therefrom, then He proceeds in delivering them to the one who seeks benefit on the way of gentleness without harshness; so when gentleness in action and subtlety and perception come together, then that perfection in knowledge and action is not conceivable except for God, exalted. As for His encompassing of subtleties and hidden matters, it cannot be detailed; rather the hidden with Him is like the apparent with no difference. And as for His gentleness in actions and subtlety in them, they are also not subject to enumeration, for one does not know subtlety in His action except one who knows the details of His actions and knows the subtleties of subtlety in them, and to the extent of the expansion of knowledge in them, the meaning of the name al-Latif expands. And as for al-Khabir, He is the One from whom inner reports do not escape; so no thing occurs in the kingdom and dominion, no particle moves or is still, no soul becomes restless or tranquil, except that He has its report; and He is in the meaning of al-'Alim, except that when knowledge is added to hidden inner things, it is called expertise (khibra) and its possessor is called Khabir.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of divine knowledge ('ilm). Al-Ghazali states that we must know that God is knower ('alim) of all existents, encompassing all creatures; nothing escapes His knowledge, not even the weight of a particle. He is truthful in His Quranic statement 'He is of all things Knowing' and He guides us to the proof of this by the verse 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Aware?' This verse is used as an argument: the act of creation itself implies knowledge of all details. Then Al-Ghazali explains the meaning of the two names mentioned in the verse: al-Latif (the Subtle) and al-Khabir (the Aware). Al-Latif means He who knows the subtleties and benefits of things and brings them to their recipients with gentleness. The perfection of such gentle action and subtle knowledge belongs only to God. Al-Khabir means He who knows inner reports; nothing happens in the entire universe without His knowledge. This is a form of knowledge applied to hidden matters. The passage emphasizes that God's knowledge is not derived from things, but rather things are derived from His knowledge. This is a key Ash'ari point: God's knowledge is eternal and causes things; it is not affected by them.

عليم
لطيف
خبير
خبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is like human knowledge, acquired from external sources.
Correction: Al-Ghazali clarifies that God's knowledge is not derived from things; rather, things are derived from His knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Aware?'
C) 'He is the First and the Last'
D) 'All that is in the heavens and the earth glorifies Him'

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is all-encompassing, not derived from creation, and is proven by the very act of creation; the names al-Latif and al-Khabir reveal the depth of His intimate awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God knows the weight of every particle and every thought you have ever had. What would you do differently today if you truly acted on that belief every moment?
?
The Creator knows you more intimately than you know yourself. What secret excuse or justification have you been holding onto that you would abandon if you realized He sees through it completely?
?
Al-Latif is the One who knows the subtleties of what is good for you and delivers it with gentleness. When was the last time you thanked Him for a blessing you did not even recognize as such? What hidden subtlety might He be sending you right now that you are ignoring?

The Second Principle: God's All-Encompassing Knowledge
Ghazali states the second principle (God's knowledge) with scriptural evidence and exposition of al-Latif and al-Khabir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge encompasses all things, is not derived from creation, and is perfectly manifested in His names al-Latif and al-Khabir.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is absolute, not learned; creation is known through His knowledge, not vice versa.

Imam al-Ghazali now presents the second foundational principle of faith: that Allah knows all things completely. He supports this with Qur'anic verses and explains the profound meanings of two divine names, al-Latif and al-Khabir. This passage deepens our understanding of how God's knowledge differs from human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Let the author's own words establish the principle.
Notice the two Qur'anic verses cited.
2
Identify the scriptural evidence
Find the two verses (6:101 and 67:14) and note how each supports the principle of God's knowledge. The first asserts it directly, the second connects creation to knowledge.
3
Understand al-Latif and al-Khabir
Pay attention to the detailed explanation of these two names. Al-Latif combines knowledge of subtle benefits with gentle action; al-Khabir is knowledge of hidden inward matters.
Note that al-Khabir is a special aspect of al-'Alim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني : العلم . العلم بأنه تعالى عالم بجميع الموجودات ، ومحيط بكل المخلوقات يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة في الأرض ولا في السماء ، صادق في قوله وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ (١) ومرشد إلى صدقه بقوله تعالى : أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ ﴾ (٢) لا أرشدك إلى الإستدلال بالخلق على العلم بأنك لا تستريب في دلالة الخلق (١) الآية (۱۰۱) من سورة الأنعام (٦) والمعنى: أنه سبحانه ظاهره وباطنه دقيق وجليله أوله وآخره ، عاقبته وخاتمته ، وهذا من حيث الكشف على أتم ما يمكن فيه بحيث لا يتصور مشاهدة وكشف أظهر منه ، ولا يكون مستفاداً من المعلومات بل تكون المعلومات مستفادة منه . (٢) الآية (١٤) من سورة الملك (٦٧) . والمراد : من يعلم دقائق المصالح وغوامضها وما دق منها وما لطف ثم يسلك في إيصالها إلى المستصلح على سبيل الرفق دون العنق ، فإذا إجتمع الرفق في الفعل واللطف والإدراك ، ثم اللطف ولا يتصور كمال ذلك في العلم والفعل إلا الله تعالى ؛ فأما إحاطته بالدقائق معنی والخفايا فلا يمكن تفصيل ذلك ، بل الخفي عنده كالجلي من غير فرق ، وأما رفقه في الأفعال ولطفه فيها فلا يدخل أيضاً تحت الحصر إذ لا يعرف اللطف في فعله إلا من عرف تفاصيل أفعاله وعرف دقائق اللطف فيها وبقدر إتساع المعرفة فيها تتسع بمعنى إسم اللطيف ؛ وأما الخبير فهو الذي لا تعزب عنه الأخبار الباطنة فلا يجري في الملك والملكوت شيء لا تتحرك ذرة ولا تسكن ولا تضطرب نفس ولا تطمئن إلا ويكون عنده خبرها وهو بمعنى العليم ، إلا أن العلم إذا أضيف الى الخفا ، الباطنة سمي خبرة ويسمى صاحبها خبيراً .
The second principle: Knowledge. The knowledge that He, Exalted, is Knower of all existent things, and encompasses all creatures; not even the weight of a particle escapes His knowledge in the earth or in the heavens. He is truthful in His statement: 'And He is of all things Knowing' (6:101), and He guides to His truthfulness by His statement, Exalted: 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Aware?' (67:14). It guides you to inference from creation to knowledge, for you have no doubt about the indication of creation. (1) Verse 101 of Surah al-An'am (6). Its meaning: that He, glorified, is manifest and hidden, subtle and grand, first and last, consequence and conclusion. This, in terms of unveiling, is at the utmost completeness possible such that no perception or unveiling more apparent than it can be conceived. It is not derived from the known things; rather, the known things are derived from it. (2) Verse 14 of Surah al-Mulk (67). Its intended meaning: He who knows the subtleties of benefits and their intricacies, both subtle and delicate, then proceeds to deliver them to the one who benefits with gentleness, not harshness. So when gentleness in action, subtlety, and perception combine—then subtlety—the perfection of that in knowledge and action is not conceivable except from Allah, Exalted. As for His encompassing of subtle meanings and hidden things, it cannot be detailed; rather, the hidden is to Him like the manifest, with no difference. As for His gentleness in actions and subtlety in them, that too cannot be encompassed, for the subtlety in His action is not known except by one who knows the details of His actions and knows the subtleties of gentleness in them. And to the extent of the breadth of knowledge in them, the meaning of the name al-Latif expands. As for al-Khabir, it is He from whom inward reports do not escape. Nothing occurs in the kingdom and dominion—no particle moves or is still, no soul is agitated or tranquil—except that He has its report. It is synonymous with al-'Alim, except that when knowledge is related to hidden inward things, it is called expertise (khibrah) and its possessor is called Khabir.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second principle of faith concerning God's attribute of knowledge. The author, Imam al-Ghazali, begins by stating the principle: that God is Knower of all existent things and encompasses all creatures; nothing escapes His knowledge. He then provides scriptural evidence from two Qur'anic verses: 'And He is of all things Knowing' (6:101) and 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Aware?' (67:14). The second verse includes two of God's names: al-Latif and al-Khabir. The author then explains the meaning of these names in detail. For al-Latif, he says it indicates God's knowledge of subtle benefits and His gentleness in delivering them. He emphasizes that the perfection of knowledge and action is exclusive to Allah. The hidden and manifest are alike to Him. For al-Khabir, he explains that it refers to God's knowledge of inward matters—nothing in the heavens and earth escapes His awareness. The passage concludes by stating that when knowledge relates to hidden inward things, it is called expertise (khibrah). The author uses this explanation to reinforce the absolute nature of God's knowledge.

الخبير
علمه تعالى بجميع الموجودات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's knowledge is like human knowledge, derived from learning.
Correction: This passage clarifies that creation derives from His knowledge, not vice versa. God's knowledge is not acquired; it is eternal and all-encompassing.

Checkpoint

C) That He learns from creation gradually.
D) That He knows only general matters, not details.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is perfect, all-encompassing, and not derived from creation; rather, all things are known through His eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson says that the hidden is to Him like the manifest, with no difference. Which areas of your life do you most try to keep hidden, and how does this truth expose the futility of concealment?
?
If God's knowledge is not learned from creation but creation is derived from His knowledge, what does that imply about your own attempts to 'inform' God through your prayers? Are your prayers a report to God or an acknowledgment of His prior knowledge?

The Third Principle: God is Living
Ghazali proves God's life as necessary from knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of life is necessarily deduced from His knowledge and power, and that His life is perfect and absolute while all other life is limited.

Core Idea
God's life is the necessary consequence of His knowledge and power.

Imam Ghazali now establishes the third principle of faith: that God is living (Hayy). This may seem obvious, but he shows that life is not an independent attribute; rather, it follows necessarily from knowledge and power. To affirm knowledge and power while denying life would lead to absurdities, even casting doubt on the life of animals and humans. This passage grounds the attribute of life in rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the argument before analyzing it.
Notice the structure: statement, deduction, absurdity, perfection, proof.
2
Identify the central deduction
Focus on the sentence: 'For whosoever's knowledge and power are established, his life is necessarily deduced.' Understand that life is a necessary consequence, not an independent attribute.
3
Consider the absurdity argument
Ghazali uses a hypothetical: if one could conceive of a powerful, knowing being without life, then one could doubt the life of animals and humans. This shows the absurdity of denying life when knowledge and power are present.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Contrast God's life with creation
Recognize that God's life is perfect and absolute (al-hayy al-kamil al-mutlaq), while the life of creatures is limited by their perception and action.
This highlights the uniqueness of God's life.
5
Review the proof
The final part states that the evidence is that God is knowing and powerful, and a knowing, powerful being must be living. Also, action cannot proceed from a dead or inanimate object, so the existence of the world as His action requires life.
This connects the attribute of life to the earlier principles of knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث : الحياة العلم بكونه عز وجل حيا (١) ، فإن من ثبت علمه وقدرته بالضرورة حياته (۲) ، ولو تصور قادر وعالم فاعل مدبر دون أن يكون حياً لجاز أن يشك في حياة الحيوانات عند ترددها في الحركات والسكنات ، بل في حياة أرباب الحرف والصناعات ، وذلك انغماس في غمرة الجهالات والضلالات (١) وهو الذي تندرج جميع المدركات تحت إدراكه وجميع الموجودات تحت فعله حتى لا يشذ عن علمه مدرك ، ولا عن فعله مفعول وذلك هو الله تعالى ، فهو الحي الكامل المطلق وكل حي سواه فحياته بقدر إدراكه وفعله وكل ذلك محصور في قلة (۲) أي أن الدليل عليه ما دلنا على كون الباري تعالى عالماً قادراً ، ومن شرط العالم القادر أن يكون حياً ، وأيضاً دلنا على أن العالم فعله ، ويستحيل صدور الفعل عن الميت والجماد
The third principle: Life. The knowledge that He, Mighty and Majestic, is Living (1). For whosoever's knowledge and power are established, his life is necessarily deduced (2). And if one could conceive of a powerful, knowing, acting, managing being without being living, then one might doubt the life of animals when they alternate in movements and stillness, indeed even the life of craftsmen and artisans. And that is immersion in the flood of ignorances and misguidances. (1) He is the One under whose perception all perceived things fall, and under whose action all existent things fall, so that no perceived thing escapes His knowledge, and no effect escapes His action. That is Allah, Exalted. He is the perfect, absolute Living One. Every living being other than Him has life according to the extent of its perception and action, and all that is limited in scantiness. (2) That is, the evidence for it is what has indicated to us that the Creator, Exalted, is Knowing and Powerful. And part of the condition of a knowing, powerful being is that it is living. Also, it has been indicated to us that His action is the world, and it is impossible for action to proceed from a dead thing or an inanimate object.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the third principle of faith: that God is Living (Hayy). The author argues that from the established attributes of knowledge and power, life is necessarily deduced. To deny life while affirming knowledge and power would be absurd, as it would even allow doubt about the life of animals and humans. The footnote further explains that God's life is perfect and absolute, encompassing all perception and action, while the life of others is limited. The proof is that we have already established that God is Knowing and Powerful, and it is a necessary condition that a knowing, powerful being must be living; action from a dead or inanimate thing is impossible. Thus, life is inseparable from God's attributes.

Key Terms
الحياة
حي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that life is an independent attribute that requires separate evidence, or that it can be denied even if knowledge and power are affirmed.
Correction: The passage shows that life is necessarily deduced from knowledge and power; to affirm knowledge and power while denying life leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
In this passage, how is God's life established?
A) Directly from scripture without reasoning.
B) Necessarily from the prior establishment of God's knowledge and power.
C) By observing that all living things have life.
D) By analogy with human life.

Key Takeaway
God's life is not an attribute to be proven independently; it is a necessary conclusion from His knowledge and power, and it is perfect and absolute, unlike the limited life of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God is Living, perfect and absolute. Does your daily life reflect that He sees every action you take and hears every word you say? What one change will you make today to live with greater awareness of His constant presence?
?
If God's life is necessary from His knowledge and power, then He is actively engaged in your life at every moment. Are you living as if that is true? Where have you been acting as if God is distant or unaware of your struggles?
?
The life of every creature is limited and scanty compared to God's perfect life. This means your own life is fragile and dependent on Him. Does this humble you? What would it mean for you to truly depend on God's life rather than relying on your own limited strength?

Fourth Principle: Will (al-Irada)
Ghazali presents the fourth principle: God has will, with rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God possesses will (irada) and why His actions are voluntary, not necessary.

Core Idea
God's will is proven by the possibility of opposites in His actions.

Imam Ghazali continues his exposition of the divine attributes by presenting the fourth principle: that God is a Willer (murid). He grounds this in a rational argument: every action of God could have been otherwise, indicating choice. In this lesson, we will examine the text and its proof, clarifying a potential misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the footnotes. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Pay attention to the footnotes; they clarify key points.
2
Identify the proof structure
The passage states a principle, then gives a proof: every action could have its opposite. Identify how this proves will.
3
Understand the footnote on terminology
The first footnote explains that the term 'murid' is not explicitly in the Qur'an but is established by consensus and verb forms. This shows how kalām uses reason alongside revelation.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why some might think God's actions are necessary. The passage shows they are voluntary because opposites are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع : الإرادة العلم بكونه تعالى مريداً لأفعاله ، فلا موجود إلا وهو مستند إلى مشيئته وصادر عن إرادته (۳) ، فهو المبدىء المعيد ، والفعال لما يريد ، وكيف(٤) لا يكون مريداً وكل فعل صدر منه أمكن أن يصدر منه ضده (٥) ، ومالا ضد له أمكن أن (٣) ذلك أن المريد لم يرد به السمع على هذه الصيغة ، وإنما ورد بصيغة الفعل ، ولكن إطلاق مريد مما ثبت بالإجماع ؛ (٤) وفي نسخة أخرى : فكيف لا يكون مريداً . (٥) يعني كل صادر عنه تعالى من الممكنات في وقت من الأوقات كان من الممكن صدور ضده فيه أي ضد ذلك الصادر بعينه في وقت آخر
The fourth principle: Will. The knowledge that He, Exalted, is Willer (murid) of His actions. No existent exists except that it is dependent on His will and proceeds from His intention (3). Thus He is the Beginner and the Repeater, the Doer of what He wills. And how (4) should He not be a Willer, while every action that proceeds from Him can possibly proceed from its opposite (5)? And that which has no opposite can possibly [not proceed?] (3) That is, the term 'Willer' (murid) has not come in the revelation in this form (the verbal noun), but it has come in the form of the verb. However, the appellation 'Willer' is established by consensus. (4) Another copy: 'Then how should He not be a Willer?' (5) Meaning: every possible thing that proceeds from Him, Exalted, at any time could possibly have its opposite proceed at that time—that is, the opposite of that very thing at another time.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth principle: that God is Willer (murid) of His actions. The author asserts that every existent depends on God's will and proceeds from His intention. He then provides a rational proof: every action that God does could have had its opposite occur instead. For things that have no opposite, the same principle of possibility applies (the text cuts off but implies that if an action had no opposite, it could still be otherwise in some way). The footnote explains that while the exact term 'murid' may not appear in the Qur'an, the meaning is established by consensus and by the use of the verb. The proof relies on the fact that if God's actions were necessary, they could not be otherwise; but since we can conceive of their opposites being possible, they must proceed from will, not necessity. Thus, God's will is an attribute distinct from power and knowledge, ensuring that His actions are voluntary.

Key Terms
الإرادة
مريد
مشيئته
ضد
الفعال لما يريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's actions are necessitated by His nature and could not be otherwise.
Correction: The passage shows that for every action, its opposite is possible, indicating choice and will, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what evidence is given to prove that God has will (irada)?
A) That God's actions are all necessary and cannot be otherwise.
B) That every action from God could possibly have its opposite occur, indicating choice.
C) That God's will is explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an as an attribute.
D) That humans have will, so God must have will by analogy.

Key Takeaway
God's will is a necessary attribute, proven by the fact that His actions could have been otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every detail of your life—from the breath you just took to the trials you fear—is a direct act of God's will, what would change in how you face tomorrow's challenges? Can you name one specific situation where you habitually forget this and instead attribute outcomes to chance or your own effort? What step will you take today to remind yourself that God willed that exact moment for a purpose?
?
The proof shows that God's actions could have been otherwise, but He chose this specific world for you. When you reflect on your past mistakes or disappointments, do you truly believe that God willed them as part of a perfect plan, or do you secretly wish He had willed differently? How does this lesson confront your trust in divine wisdom?
?
If God's will is the source of every existent, then your own will and choices are also dependent on His. Does this truth humble you or trouble you? Are you willing to submit your desires to His, knowing that His will is not arbitrary but based on perfect wisdom? What is one area of your life where you struggle to say, 'I accept Your will over mine'?

The Third Origin: Life
Imam Ghazali defines the attribute of life and gives its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life is necessarily entailed by Allah's knowledge and power, and how this connects to His being the active Creator.

Core Idea
Life is not proven separately; it follows necessarily from knowledge and power.

In this lesson, we examine Imam Ghazali's succinct argument for the divine attribute of life. Rather than offering an independent proof, he demonstrates that life is a necessary condition for a knowing and powerful being. The text also contrasts Allah's absolute, perfect life with the limited life of creatures, and warns against the absurdity of imagining a dead Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the entire Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the statement to the proof and the warning.
Pay attention to the footnotes which are part of the original text.
2
Identify the proof structure
Find where Ghazali states that life is entailed by knowledge and power (فإن من ثبت علمه وقدرته بالضرورة حياته). Then note the reductio ad absurdum against imagining a powerful agent without life.
The proof is not direct but based on necessary conditions.
3
Understand the footnotes (annotations 1 and 2)
Footnote 1 describes the absolute nature of Allah's life: He perceives and acts upon all. Footnote 2 clarifies the proof: the world is His act, and an act cannot come from a dead being.
These annotations are integral to the argument.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think life must be proven from scratch, but Ghazali shows it is derived from already established attributes (knowledge and power). Consider why this logic is important in kalam.
Misconception: life is an independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث : الحياة
العلم بكونه عز وجل حيا (١) ، فإن من ثبت علمه وقدرته بالضرورة حياته (۲) ، ولو تصور قادر وعالم فاعل مدبر دون أن يكون حياً لجاز أن يشك في حياة الحيوانات عند ترددها في الحركات والسكنات ، بل في حياة أرباب الحرف والصناعات ، وذلك انغماس في غمرة الجهالات والضلالات
(۱) وهو الذي تندرج جميع المدركات تحت إدراكه وجميع الموجودات تحت فعله حتى لا يشذ عن علمه مدرك ، ولا عن فعله مفعول وذلك هو الله تعالى ، فهو الحي الكامل المطلق وكل حي سواه فحياته بقدر إدراكه وفعله وكل ذلك محصور في قلة
(۲) أي أن الدليل عليه ما دلنا على كون الباري تعالى عالماً قادراً ، ومن شرط العالم القادر أن يكون حياً ، وأيضاً دلنا على أن العالم فعله ، ويستحيل صدور الفعل عن الميت والجماد
The Third Origin: Life. Knowledge that He, Mighty and Majestic, is Living (1). For whoever's knowledge and power are established, his life is necessarily entailed (2). If a powerful, knowing, acting, managing being could be imagined without being alive, then it would be permissible to doubt the life of animals in their movements and rest, and even the life of craftsmen and artisans. That would be immersion in the depths of ignorance and misguidance. (1) He is the One under whose perception all perceptibles are subsumed, and under whose action all existents are subsumed, so that no perceptible escapes His knowledge, and no thing acted upon escapes His action. That is Allah, the Exalted. He is the perfect, absolute Living. Every living being other than Him has life according to its perception and action, and all that is limited in scarcity. (2) That is, the proof for it is what has indicated to us that the Creator, Exalted, is Knowing and Powerful. And it is a condition of the knowing, powerful being that he be alive. Also, it has indicated to us that the world is His act, and it is impossible for an act to issue from a dead or inanimate thing.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof for the divine attribute of life. The author states the third fundamental belief: that Allah is alive. He immediately gives the proof: since we have already established that He is knowing and powerful, and it is a necessary condition of a knowing and powerful being that it be alive, therefore He is alive. The author then warns against the absurdity of imagining a powerful, knowing agent who is not alive, for if that were possible, one could doubt the life of animals and humans, which is clearly false. The first footnote explains the absolute nature of Allah's life: all perceptibles are under His perception and all existents under His action, so no perceptible escapes His knowledge and no thing escapes His action. He is the perfect, absolute living being, while all other living beings have limited life based on their perception and action. The second footnote clarifies the proof: the same evidence that proved His knowledge and power also proves His life, because a knowing and powerful being must be alive, and it is impossible for an act to issue from something dead or inanimate.

Key Terms
حياة
حي
علم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the attribute of life must be proven independently, like knowledge and power.
Correction: Ghazali shows that life is not proven separately but is necessarily entailed by knowledge and power. Since we have already established those, life follows directly.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Ghazali prove that Allah is alive?
A) By direct revelation
B) By the necessity that a knowing and powerful being be alive
C) By analogy with animals
D) By negating death

Key Takeaway
Life is a necessary attribute of Allah, entailed by His knowledge and power, and without it the world could not be His act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah's life is not optional but necessary from His knowledge and power. Is there any area in your life where you treat Allah as merely 'knowing and powerful' but not truly alive and present? How will you change that today?
?
The passage warns that denying the entailment could lead to doubting the life of animals and humans. When you look at the world, do you truly see His life reflected in the life of every creature? What would it mean for you to live as someone whose own life is entirely dependent on the Living Allah?
?
Allah's life is perfect and absolute, while yours is limited and fleeting. What would change in your daily routine if you truly internalised that every breath you take is a gift from al-Hayy? Commit to one specific act today that acknowledges His life over yours.

The Fourth Origin: Will
Imam Ghazali proves the attribute of will for Allah through rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for Allah's attribute of will and why knowledge alone cannot specify which act occurs.

Core Idea
Allah's will specifies which of two equally possible acts becomes reality.

Imam Ghazali now turns to the fourth fundamental belief: that Allah is willing (murid) for His actions. He presents a rational argument that does not rely solely on scripture: because every act could have been otherwise, a specifying factor beyond power and knowledge must exist. This factor is the divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Ghazali builds his argument step by step.
Pay attention to the use of 'مريد' and 'إرادة'.
2
Identify the core argument
Ghazali argues that for every act, either an opposite could have occurred, or the same act could have occurred at a different time. Power is equally related to both possibilities, so an additional attribute (will) is needed to specify which one is actualized.
3
Understand the refutation of knowledge
Ghazali refutes the idea that knowledge alone could specify: if knowledge sufficed, then power would be unnecessary, which is clearly wrong. This strengthens the need for a distinct attribute of will.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the meaning of will
Will is defined as a real attribute that specifies which possible thing occurs at a specific time. This distinguishes it from mere knowledge or power.
The footnotes clarify that the term 'Willing' is derived from verbs and established by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع : الإرادة
العلم بكونه تعالى مريداً لأفعاله ، فلا موجود إلا وهو مستند إلى مشيئته وصادر عن إرادته (۳) ، فهو المبدىء المعيد ، والفعال لما يريد ، وكيف(٤) لا يكون مريداً وكل فعل صدر منه أمكن أن يصدر منه ضده (٥) ، ومالا ضد له أمكن أن يصدر منه ذلك بعينه قبله أو بعده ، والقدرة تناسب الضدين والوقتين مناسبة واحدة ، فلا بد من إرادة صارفة للقدرة إلى أحد المقدورين (١) ، ولو أغنى العلم عن الإرادة في تخصيص المعلوم حتى يقال إنما وجد (۲) في الوقت الذي سبق العلم بوجوده ، لجاز أن يغنى عن القدرة حتى يقال : وجد بغير قدرة ، لأنه سبق العلم بوجوده فيه(٣).
(۳) ذلك أن المريد لم يرد به السمع على هذه الصيغة ، وإنما ورد بصيغة الفعل ، ولكن إطلاق مريد مما ثبت بالإجماع ؛
(٤) وفي نسخة أخرى : فكيف لا يكون مريداً .
(٥) يعني كل صادر عنه تعالى من الممكنات في وقت من الأوقات كان من الممكن صدور ضده فيه أي ضد ذلك الصادر بعينه في وقت آخر
(١) فتخصيصه بصدوره في ذلك الوقت دون ذلك الممكن الآخر ودون ما قبل ذلك الوقت وما بعده لا بد من كونه يصرف القدرة المناسبة للضدين والوقتين على السواء عن إيجاد ذلك الممكن من غير ذلك الوقت أو إيجاد غيره بدله في ذلك الوقت إلى تخصيص ذلك الممكن دون غيره بذلك الوقت المخصوص ، ولا نعني بالإرادة إلا ذلك المعنى المخصص وهو صفة حقيقية قائمة بذاته توجب تخصيص المقدور دون غيره بخصوص وقت إيجاده دون ما قبله ، وما بعده من الأوقات
(۲) وفي نسخة أخرى : إنما يوجد في الوقت ... الخ
(٣) وهذه الجملة أوردها إمام الحرمين في سياق الرد على الكعبي من المعتزلة
The Fourth Origin: Will. Knowledge that He, Exalted, is Willing for His actions. For no existent exists except it is dependent on His will and issued from His intention (3). He is the Beginner and the Returner, and the Doer of what He wills. How (4) could He not be Willing, while every act that issues from Him could have issued from Him its opposite (5), and what has no opposite could have issued from Him that very thing before or after? Power relates equally to the two opposites and the two times, so an will directing power to one of the two predestined things is necessary (1). And if knowledge sufficed for the specification of the known, so that it is said that it only exists (2) at the time which knowledge preceded its existence, then it would be permissible for power to be dispensed with, so that it is said: it exists without power, because knowledge preceded its existence at that time (3). (3) That is, 'the Willing' was not revealed in this form from the audial [revelation], but rather came in the form of the verb; however, the absolute term 'Willing' is established by consensus. (4) In another copy: 'How could He not be Willing?' (5) Meaning: every possible thing issuing from Him, Exalted, at a time, it was possible for its opposite to issue at that time, i.e., the opposite of that very thing at another time. (1) So its specification by its occurrence at that time, rather than that other possible thing and rather than before that time and after it, must involve that it directs the power which is equally related to the two opposites and the two times away from bringing that possible thing into existence at not that time or bringing another in its place at that time, toward specifying that possible thing, not other than it, at that particular time. And we mean by 'will' nothing but that specifying meaning, which is a real attribute subsisting in His essence that necessitates the specification of the predestined thing, not other than it, at the particular time of its bringing into existence, not before it, nor after it. (2) In another copy: 'It only exists at the time... etc.' (3) This sentence was cited by Imam al-Haramayn in the context of refuting al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the divine attribute of will. The author states the fourth fundamental belief: that Allah is willing (murid) for His actions. He asserts that every existent depends on His will. Then he presents the rational proof: For every act that proceeds from Allah, it could have been its opposite (if the act has an opposite), or if it has no opposite, the act itself could have occurred at a different time. Power relates equally to both opposites and both times, so there must be a will that specifies which one is actualized. The author then refutes the objection that knowledge might suffice for specification: if knowledge alone sufficed to determine that something occurs at a specific time, then power would also be unnecessary, which is absurd—since Allah's power is established. The footnotes clarify that the term 'Willing' is not explicitly in the Qur'an as an attribute noun but is derived from verbs and is established by consensus (ijma). Another variant reading is noted. A long footnote defines will as a real attribute that specifies which possible thing occurs at which time. Finally, the author attributes the refutation of al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazilite) to Imam al-Haramayn.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
قدرة
تخصيص
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's knowledge is sufficient to determine why things happen at specific times, without need for will.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if knowledge sufficed, then power could be dispensed with, which is absurd. So will is necessary as a specifying attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Ghazali argue that Allah must have will?
A) Because the Qur'an says so
B) Because otherwise the world would not exist
C) Because power relates equally to opposites and times, so a specifying will is needed
D) Because knowledge alone cannot create

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is an essential attribute that specifies which possible act is actualized at any given time, distinct from power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something unexpected happens in your life, do you truly affirm that it occurred by Allah's will and not by chance? How does this belief affect your reaction to it?
?
You know that Allah has power over all things. Yet, have you ever felt that your own choices are not subject to Allah's will? How does this lesson challenge that feeling and push you to submit to His decree?
?
If Allah's will specifies every act, what is the role of your own will? Does this make you feel powerless or responsible? How can you reconcile your freedom with Allah's absolute will in your daily actions?

The Fifth Origin: Hearing and Sight as Divine Attributes
Ghazali defines and proves the divine attributes of hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are real, existential attributes that perceive all things without organs or physical processes.

Core Idea
Allah hears and sees all things without a body, limb, or organ.

Imam Ghazali now turns to two attributes that are often misunderstood through anthropomorphism. He establishes that Allah is Hearing and Seeing in a way befitting His majesty, not like creatures. This lesson presents the proof from universal perception and defines these attributes as existential qualities of His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice how Ghazali uses the examples of the ant and the whisper to illustrate Allah's perception.
2
Identify the proof for hearing and sight
Find where Ghazali argues that nothing escapes Allah's hearing and sight. What examples does he give? How does he conclude that He must be Hearing and Seeing?
The proof is based on the fact that Allah perceives all things, therefore He has these attributes.
3
Understand the negation of anthropomorphism
Notice how Ghazali explicitly states that hearing and sight are without organs (bila jariha, hadaqa, udhun). Compare with human hearing and sight which depend on physical mechanisms.
This guards against thinking of Allah as having body parts.
4
Learn the definitions of hearing and sight
Focus on the definitions: hearing is an existential attribute that perceives every audible thing; sight is an existential attribute that perceives every visible thing. Write these definitions in your own words.
Existential attribute (sifa wujudiyya) means a real attribute, not merely relational.
5
Reflect on the key terms
Review the terms: سمع (hearing), بصر (sight), سميع (Hearing), بصير (Seeing), صفة وجودية (existential attribute). Understand how they are used in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الخامس : السمع والبصر
العلم بأنه تعالى سميع بصير (٤) لا يعزب عن رؤيته هواجس الضمير وخفايا الوهم والتفكير (٥) ، ولا يشذ عن سمعه صوت دبيب النملة السوداء في الليلة الظلماء على الصخرة الصماء (٦) ، وكيف لا يكون سميعاً بصيراً والسمع والبصر
(٤) بلا جارحة وحدقة ولا اذن كما أنه تعالى عليم بلا دماغ ، وقلب ، اليس سمعه كسمع المخلوق الذي هو قوة مودعة في مقعر الصماخ يتوقف إدراكها للأصوات على حصول الهواء الموصل إلى الحاسة وتأثر الحاسة ولا كبصر المخلوق الذي هو قوّة مودعة في العصبتين المجوفتين الخارجتين من الدماغ ، بل المراد بالسمع صفة وجودية قائمة بالذات شأنها إدراك كل مسموع وإن خفى ؛ والمراد بالبصر صفة وجودية قائمة بالذات شأنها إدراك كل مبصر ، وإن لطف
(٥) والمراد بالوهم : الهواجس وما يخطر بالبال والوهم بمعناه .
والمراد من التفكير : ما خفي عنه وهو مصدر فكره مشدداً إذ أورده في فكره
(٦) والمعنى المراد : أن سمعه سبحانه منزه من أن يتطرق إليه الحدثان ومهما نزهت السميع عن تغير =
The Fifth Origin: Hearing and Sight. Knowledge that He, Exalted, is Hearing, Seeing (4). The whispers of conscience and the hidden things of illusion and thinking (5) do not escape His vision, and the sound of the crawling of a black ant on a solid rock in the dark night does not escape His hearing (6). How could He not be Hearing, Seeing, while hearing and sight... (4) Without a limb, pupil, or ear, just as He, Exalted, is Knowing without a brain or heart. Is His hearing like the hearing of a creature, which is a power deposited in the hollow of the ear canal, its perception of sounds depending on the occurrence of air transmitting to the sense and the sense being affected? Nor like the sight of a creature, which is a power deposited in the two hollow nerves emerging from the brain. Rather, what is meant by 'hearing' is an existential attribute subsisting in the essence, whose function is to perceive every audible thing, even if faint; and what is meant by 'sight' is an existential attribute subsisting in the essence, whose function is to perceive every visible thing, even if subtle. (5) And what is meant by 'illusion' (al-wahm): the whispers and what occurs to the mind, and 'illusion' in its meaning. And what is meant by 'thinking' (al-tafkir): what is hidden from it, and it is the verbal noun of 'fakkar' (emphasized) when he brings it into his thought. (6) And the intended meaning: that His hearing, glory be to Him, is transcendent from being subject to temporal occurrences. Whenever you declare the Hearer free from change... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the attributes of hearing and sight for Allah. The author states the fifth fundamental belief: that Allah is Hearing (Sami') and Seeing (Basir). The proof is that nothing, however hidden or faint, escapes His perception: the secrets of conscience and the crawling of an ant. He then asks how He could not be Hearing and Seeing, since hearing and sight are attributes of perfection. The first footnote clarifies that these attributes are without organs (la jariha, hadaqa, udhun) just as He is Knowing without a brain or heart. Allah's hearing is not like the hearing of creatures, which depends on physical processes. Rather, hearing and sight are existential attributes (sifa wujuudiyya) subsisting in His essence that perceive all audible and visible things. The second footnote explains the terms 'illusion' (wahm) and 'thinking' (tafkir). The third footnote emphasizes the transcendence of His hearing from temporal change, but it is cut off.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
سميع
بصير
صفة وجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's hearing and sight are like human hearing and sight, involving organs and physical processes.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that Allah's hearing and sight are without limbs, pupils, or ears, and they are not dependent on air or nerves. They are existential attributes that perceive all things directly, not via physical means.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ghazali, what distinguishes Allah's hearing and sight from human hearing and sight?
A) They are stronger but function similarly.
B) They are without organs and not dependent on physical processes.
C) They only perceive loud sounds and bright colors.
D) They are metaphorical and not real attributes.

Key Takeaway
Allah's hearing and sight are real existential attributes that perceive all things, without any physical organs or similarities to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The ant on the rock in the dark night represents the most insignificant, hidden action. Is there an area of your life you treat as if Allah does not see or hear it? What would it mean for you to stop treating that area as hidden from Him?
?
Ghazali contrasts Allah's hearing/sight with human senses that depend on physical organs. When you feel alone or unheard by people, do you truly act as if Allah's hearing is always present? How would your response to loneliness change if you remembered that Allah hears you more clearly than any human hears?

The Fifth Principle: Divine Hearing and Sight as Perfections
Ghazali defines the fifth principle: Allah's hearing and sight as necessary perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers divine hearing and sight necessary perfections and how he argues for them using reason and the example of Prophet Ibrahim.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight are perfections that cannot be absent from the Creator.

Ghazali presents the fifth principle of Islamic belief: that Allah is Hearing and Seeing. He begins by describing the reach of these attributes — nothing escapes His sight or hearing. Then he offers a rational argument: if hearing and sight are perfections, how could the Creator lack what He gives to creatures? Finally, he invokes the example of Prophet Ibrahim to show that denying these attributes would undermine the very proof of monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the original Arabic and the English translation side by side. Notice the powerful imagery of a black ant on a black rock in a dark night to emphasize Allah's encompassing perception.
2
Identify the central argument
Ghazali argues that hearing and sight are perfections; therefore, the Creator must possess them to a greater degree than creatures. Find the rhetorical questions that make this point.
The questions are: 'How can the creature be more perfect than the Creator?' and 'How can the division be balanced?'
3
Analyze the Ibrahim example
Why does Ghazali use Ibrahim's argument against idolaters? He shows that if Allah lacked hearing and sight, the same criticism would apply, making Ibrahim's proof invalid.
Ibrahim said to his father: 'Why do you worship that which cannot hear, nor see, nor avail you in anything?'
4
Synthesize the lesson
Now that you've understood the passage, explain in your own words why these attributes are necessary for Allah.
Focus on the perfection argument: since hearing and sight are perfections, the All-Perfect Creator must possess them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الخامس : السمع والبصر
العلم بأنه تعالى سميع بصير لا يعزب عن رؤيته هواجس الضمير وخفايا الوهم والتفكير، ولا يشذ عن سمعه صوت دبيب النملة السوداء في الليلة الظلماء على الصخرة الصماء، وكيف لا يكون سميعاً بصيراً والسمع والبصر كمال لا محالة وليس بنقص؟ فكيف يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق الصانع؟ والمصنوع أسنى وأتم من الصانع؟ أو كيف تعتدل القسمة مهما وقع النقص في جهته والكمال في خلقه وصنعته؟ أو كيف تستقيم حجة إبراهيم على أبيه إذ كان يعبد الأصنام جهلاً وغيا، فقال له: لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْكَ شَيْئًا؟ ولو انقلب ذلك عليه في معبوده لأضحت حجته داحضة ودلالته ساقطة ولم يصدق قوله تعالى:
The fifth principle: Hearing and Sight. The knowledge that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing; nothing of the whisperings of conscience or the hidden thoughts of imagination and reflection escapes His sight, and the sound of the crawling of a black ant on a black rock in a dark night is not hidden from His hearing. And how could He not be Hearing, Seeing, while hearing and sight are undoubtedly perfection, not deficiency? So how can the creature be more perfect than the Creator, the Maker? And the made thing be more sublime and more complete than the Maker? Or how can the division be balanced whenever deficiency occurs in His side and perfection in His creation and handiwork? Or how can the argument of Ibrahim against his father stand, when he was worshipping idols out of ignorance and error, and said to him: 'Why do you worship that which cannot hear, nor see, nor avail you in anything?' And if that were turned back upon Him regarding His worshipped object, His argument would become invalid, His proof unsound, and His statement would not be verified.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth principle of belief: affirming that Allah possesses the attributes of hearing (sam‘) and sight (baṣar). It opens by stating that nothing escapes His sight—not the innermost thoughts or subtle imaginings—and nothing escapes His hearing, even the sound of a black ant crawling on a black rock in a dark night. The author then argues that these attributes are perfections (kamaal) and not deficiencies. Since the Creator is greater than the creature, it would be absurd for the creature to have these perfections while the Creator lacks them. The passage uses the example of the Prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) pleading with his father, who worshipped idols that cannot hear or see. If Allah Himself were devoid of hearing and sight, Ibrahim’s argument would be refuted and his proof would collapse. Thus, the attributes of hearing and sight are essential for the true deity and are affirmed for Allah in a manner befitting His majesty, without anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
سَمِيع
بَصِير
كَمَال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's hearing and sight are like human senses requiring physical organs
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes they are perfections free from bodily limitations, befitting Allah's majesty.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali use the argument of Ibrahim (AS) against his father?
A) To show that Ibrahim’s father was wrong to worship idols
B) To prove that if Allah lacked hearing and sight, Ibrahim’s argument would invalidate itself
C) To demonstrate that idols are deaf and blind
D) To compare Allah to idols

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are necessary perfections, affirmed by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah hears every whisper and sees every hidden thought. What is one secret sin or intention you have been hiding, and will you bring it before Him now in sincere repentance?
?
If hearing and sight are perfections, and you believe in Allah's perfection, how does that change the way you speak or act when you think no one is watching?
?
Ibrahim used the inability of idols to hear or see to prove they were not gods. Do you ever act as if Allah does not see or hear you? When will you truly live in the constant awareness that He sees and hears everything?

The Necessity of Divine Hearing and Sight
Ghazali proves divine hearing and sight as attributes of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali argues that God must possess hearing and sight as attributes of perfection, and how this differs from human sensory organs.

Core Idea
Denying God's hearing and sight would make a creature more perfect than the Creator.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-Aqa'id presents a rational proof for divine hearing and sight. Using rhetorical questions, the argument of Abraham (peace be upon him), and an analogy to other divine attributes, Ghazali establishes that these are necessary attributes of perfection. The goal is to affirm these attributes while maintaining God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the logical flow sink in before analysis.
2
Identify the three main arguments
Notice the three parts: the perfection argument (creature vs Creator), the Abrahamic argument (idols vs God), and the analogy to other attributes (acting without limbs).
These form a cumulative proof.
3
Understand the 'perfection' argument
Why would it be absurd if a creature had a perfection that the Creator lacks? Reflect on the implications.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Ghazali emphasizes that God sees without a pupil and hears without an ear. How does this preserve His transcendence?
Important to avoid anthropomorphism.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق الصانع ؟ والمصنوع أسنى وأتم من صنعته ؟ أو كيف تعتدل القسمة مهما وقع النقص في جهته والكمال في خلقه ؟ أو كيف تستقيم حجة إبراهيم على أبيه إذ كان يعبد الأصنام جهلاً وغيا فقال له : لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْكَ شَيْئاً ؟ ولو انقلب ذلك عليه في معبوده لأضحت حجته داحضة ودلالته ساقطة . وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه . فهو تعالى أحق بالإتصاف بهما من المخلوق . وكما عقل كونه فاعلاً بلا جارحة ، وعالماً بلا قلب ودماغ ، فليعقل كونه بصيراً بلا حدقة ، وسميعاً بلا أذن ، إذ لا فرق بينهما.
So how can the creature be more perfect than the Creator, the Maker? And the manufactured thing more excellent and more complete than its workmanship? Or how can the division be balanced whenever imperfection occurs on His side and perfection on His creation? Or how can the argument of Abraham against his father stand when he worshipped idols out of ignorance and error, saying to him: 'Why do you worship that which does not hear, does not see, and does not avail you anything?' And if that were turned against his deity, his argument would become invalid and his proof would fall. And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people. So He, exalted, is more deserving of being described with both (hearing and sight) than the creature. And just as it is understood that He is an agent without a limb, and knowing without a heart and brain, so let it be understood that He is seeing without a pupil, and hearing without an ear, for there is no difference between them.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine hearing and sight. The author begins by asking rhetorical questions: How can a creature be more perfect than the Creator? If God lacked hearing and sight, then a creature possessing them would be more perfect, which is absurd. He then invokes the argument of Abraham (peace be upon him) against his idol-worshipping father: Abraham criticized idols for lacking hearing and sight. If God Himself lacked these attributes, Abraham's argument would backfire. The author concludes that God is more deserving of these attributes than any creature. Finally, he draws an analogy: just as we accept that God acts without limbs and knows without organs, we must accept that He sees without a pupil and hears without an ear, as there is no difference in principle.

بصر
صفات الكمال
إبراهيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine hearing and sight are similar to human senses involving physical organs.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God hears without an ear and sees without a pupil, emphasizing that these attributes are perfect and beyond physical limitations, unlike human senses.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for affirming hearing and sight as attributes of God?
A) They are mentioned in the Quran without explanation.
B) Denying them would make a creature more perfect than the Creator.
C) They can be proven by empirical observation.
D) They are necessary for God to communicate with prophets.

Key Takeaway
God must possess hearing and sight as attributes of perfection, but in a manner unlike creation—without organs and beyond human comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God's hearing and sight are without organs and unlike anything we know. Does that truth comfort you or make you uneasy? Are you willing to submit to a God whose attributes transcend your comprehension, even when you cannot fully grasp them? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your tawheed?

Divine Speech: Eternal Attribute, Not Sound
Ghazali defines divine speech as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, not composed of sounds or letters, and that true speech is the speech of the soul.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal attribute, not created sounds or letters.

The sixth principle of Islamic creed concerns divine speech. Ghazali immediately clarifies that Allah's speech is not like human speech—it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. This passage introduces a key distinction between the inner reality of speech (kalam al-nafs) and the physical sounds and letters that merely point to it. Understanding this distinction is essential for avoiding anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the strong negation: 'not sound nor letter'.
The passage uses a comparison with existence to emphasize incomparability.
2
Identify the definition of divine speech
Ghazali states that speech is 'an attribute subsisting in His essence'. Reflect on what this means—an eternal, non-created quality of Allah.
3
Understand the 'speech of the soul' concept
The passage introduces 'kalam al-nafs' as true speech. Sounds and letters are only created indicators. This distinguishes inner meaning from physical expression.
This concept is crucial for later debates on the Qur'an.
4
Apply the principle of incomparability
Just as Allah's existence is unlike any other, His speech is unlike any other. This protects tawhid and avoids likening Allah to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السادس : الكلام أنه سبحانه وتعالى متكلم بكلام ، وهو وصف قائم بذاته ، ليس بصوت ولا حرف ، بل لا يشبه كلامه كلام غيره ، كما لا يشبه وجوده وجود غيره ؛ والكلام بالحقيقة كلام النفس ، وإنما الأصوات قطعت حروفاً للدلالات.
The sixth principle: Speech. That He, glorified and exalted, is speaking with speech, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence, not sound nor letter; rather, His speech does not resemble the speech of others, just as His existence does not resemble the existence of others; and speech in reality is the speech of the soul, while sounds are cut letters for indications.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth principle of Islamic creed concerning divine speech. The author states that God is speaking (mutakallim) with a speech that is an attribute subsisting in His essence. This speech is not composed of sounds or letters, which are accidental and created. Rather, it is an eternal attribute. The author emphasizes that God's speech is incomparable to human speech, just as His existence is incomparable. Then he explains that true speech is 'the speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs), i.e., the inner meaning, while the sounds and letters we utter are merely created signs that indicate that inner speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is composed of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that divine speech is an eternal attribute without sound or letter; sounds and letters are created signs that indicate inner speech.

Checkpoint

A) Sounds and letters.
B) The speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs).
C) The words revealed in scripture.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, entirely unlike created speech, and true speech is the inner meaning (kalam al-nafs), not physical sounds or letters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says Allah's speech does not resemble any other speech. Does your reverence for the Qur'an reflect this uniqueness, or do you approach it like any other book? What will you change starting today?

The Hearing-Seeing Analogy for Divine Speech
Ghazali uses the analogy of seeing God to defend hearing speech without sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that if we accept seeing God in the afterlife without a body or color, we must also accept hearing divine speech without sound or letter.

Core Idea
If you accept seeing God without form, accept hearing His speech without sound.

Ghazali begins by addressing someone who finds it impossible that Moses heard speech without sound or letters. He cleverly turns the tables: if you believe you will see God in the afterlife without a body or color—something you have never experienced—then why not also accept hearing speech without sound? This analogy trains the mind to accept divine realities beyond physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening challenge
Notice how Ghazali frames the objection: someone finds it unlikely that Moses heard speech without sound or letter. He calls that person 'without a guide'.
The phrase 'لا هادي له' indicates a strong criticism.
2
Identify the parallel argument
Ghazali compares hearing speech without sound to seeing God without body or color. Both are accepted in Islamic theology.
Seeing God in the afterlife is a Sunni doctrine, so the analogy uses a shared belief.
3
Understand the logical move
If you already accept one miraculous perception (sight of the non-physical), you should accept another (hearing the non-physical).
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that divine speech is not like human speech; it is an attribute of God, not a physical phenomenon.
This connects to the broader lesson on divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هاد (۱) ، ومن استبعد أن يسمع موسى عليه السلام في الدنيا كلاماً ليس بصوت ولا حرف فليستنكر أن يرى في الآخرة موجوداً ليس بجسم ولا لون (۲) وإن عقل أن يرى ما ليس بلون ولا جسم ولا قدر ولا كمية ، وهو إلى الآن لم ير غيره فليعقل في حاسة السمع ما عقله في حاسة البصر (۳) وإن عقل أن يكون له علم واحد هو علم بجميع الموجودات ، فليعقل صفة واحدة للذات هو كلام بجميع ما دل عليه بالعبارات(٤) ؛ وإن عقل كون السموات السبع وكون الجنة والنار مكتوبة في ورقة صغيرة ومحفوظة في مقدار(ه) ذرة من القلب وأن كل ذلك مرئي في مقدار عدسة الحدقة من من غير أن تحل ذات ـ السموات والارض والجنة والنار في الحدقة والقلب والورقة ، فليعقل كون الكلام مقروءاً بالألسنة ، محفوظا في القلوب ، مكتوبا في المصاحف(٦) من غير حلول ذات الكلام فيها ، إذ لو حلت بكتاب الله ذات الكلام في الورق(٧) لحل (۱) ليس له من هادٍ يرشده إلى سلوك سبيل السداد (۲) ولا قابل للحوادث ، وأن المقصود نفي الكيفية على كل حال ، وكذلك إذا إستبعدوا كيف سمع جبريل عليه السلام والمؤمنون غداً كيف يسمعون ؟ فالجواب سمع كلاماً ليس بحرف ولا صوت من متكلم حتى ليس له لسان وشفه ؛ (۳) يعني فليعقل سماع ماليس بصوت ، وهو لا يكون إلا بطريق خرق العادة كما نبه عليه الباقلاني (٤) من أمر ونهى وأخبار ، وقد جاز في الشاهد أن يكون الشيء الواحد أمراً ونهياً وخبراً وإستخباراً فكذلك يجوز في الغائب ولم يكن مستحيلاً ، وهذه العبارات مخلوقة لأنها أصوات وهي أعراض سميت تلك العبارات كلام الله لدلالتها عليه وتأديه بها ، والإختلاف في العبارات المؤدية لا الكلام (٥) وفي نسخة أخرى : في تعداد ذرة من القلب ... الخ (٦) وفي نسخة أخرى : مكتوباً في المصاحف بالأحبار المتنوعة من غير حلول ذات الكلام فيها (۷) كلمة ( في الورق ) لم توجد في النسخ الأخرى ١٨٤
A guide (1). And whoever finds it unlikely that Moses (peace be upon him) heard in this world speech that is not a sound nor a letter, then let him find it unlikely that he will see in the afterlife an existent that is not a body nor a color (2). And if he understands that he sees what is not a color, not a body, not a dimension, and not a quantity, and he until now has seen nothing else, then let him understand in the sense of hearing what he understood in the sense of sight (3). And if he understands that He has one knowledge which is knowledge of all existents, then let him understand one attribute of the essence which is speech comprising all that is indicated by expressions (4). And if he understands that the seven heavens, Paradise, and Hellfire are written in a small sheet and preserved in the amount (5) of a speck from the heart, and that all of that is visible in the amount of a lens of the pupil without the essence of the heavens, earth, Paradise, and Hellfire entering into the pupil, heart, and sheet, then let him understand that speech is read by tongues, preserved in hearts, and written in manuscripts (6) without the essence of speech entering into them. For if the essence of speech had entered into the Book of God in the paper (7), it would have entered. (1) He has no guide to show him the path of rectitude. (2) And not receptive to accidents, and the intent is the negation of modality in any case; similarly, if they find it unlikely how Gabriel heard and the believers tomorrow will hear, the answer is that he heard speech not a letter nor a sound from a speaker who has no tongue nor lip. (3) Meaning: let him understand hearing what is not a sound, and that only occurs by way of a break in custom, as al-Baqillani indicated. (4) Of command, prohibition, and reports; and it is permissible in the empirical world that one thing be a command, a prohibition, a report, and an interrogation. So likewise it is permissible in the unseen and is not impossible. And these expressions are created because they are sounds and accidents; those expressions are called the speech of God because they indicate and communicate it, and the difference is in the expressions that communicate, not in the speech. (5) And in another copy: 'in the count of a speck from the heart' etc. (6) And in another copy: 'written in the manuscripts with diverse inks without the essence of speech entering into them.' (7) The word 'in the paper' is not found in other copies. 184
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents analogical arguments to demonstrate that God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds. It opens by addressing someone who finds it unlikely that Moses heard speech without sound or letter; the author argues that such a person should equally find it unlikely that God will be seen in the afterlife without body or color, yet the latter is accepted by Muslims. Using parallelism, the author says: if one can conceive of seeing something without body or color (i.e., God in the afterlife), then one should also conceive of hearing something without sound (i.e., divine speech). Next, the author uses the analogy of God's knowledge: just as God has one knowledge that encompasses all things, He has one speech attribute that commands, prohibits, reports, and interrogates all at once. This is not impossible, since in the observable world, one statement can have multiple functions. Then, a third analogy: if one accepts that the vast heavens, Paradise, and Hellfire can be written on a small sheet of paper and seen in a tiny pupil without physically entering those mediums, then one should also accept that the Quran is read by tongues, preserved in hearts, and written in manuscripts without God's literal speech entering them. The footnote (4) clarifies that these expressions are created sounds and accidents, called 'God's speech' because they indicate and convey it, and the variation is in the expressions, not the eternal speech. Footnote (6) reiterates that the essence of speech does not enter the manuscripts.

Key Terms
سماع
بصر
كلام
جسم
لون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech must be audible like human speech, with letters and sounds.
Correction: Ghazali shows that just as God is seen without body or color, His speech is heard without sound or letter.

Checkpoint
What famous unseen event does Ghazali use to parallel hearing speech without sound?
A) Creation of the heavens
B) Seeing God in the afterlife
C) Resurrection of the dead
D) Splitting of the moon

Key Takeaway
Acceptance of one miracle (seeing God) logically requires acceptance of another (hearing His speech without sound).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that you will see Allah in the afterlife without form or color. Why then do you struggle to accept that He spoke to Moses without sound? What does this reveal about your concept of God?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever feel that you are hearing Allah's speech without sound? How can this lesson reshape your relationship with the divine word?
?
Have you ever rejected a theological truth simply because it didn't fit your sensory experience? How does this analogy call you to expand your understanding of Allah's attributes?
